==PAGE:3==
Summary
The aim of this thesis is to showcase the development of a complete guidance and control system based
on data-driven system identification. The guidance and control system enables a digital twin of the ferry
M/F Copenhagen to autonomously perform a complex turning manoeuvre and to follow a dynamically
generated route. At first, a data-driven model is identified using support vector regression. The model
is used in the guidance system, which consists of a combination of RRT* and a dynamical optimisation
approach. The guidance system is used to create a reference for the control system to follow. Several
controllers are explored in this thesis. Among them are input-to-state linearisation control schemes,
integrator backstepping, guided motion control and switching control.
The controllers are used in
conjunction with the control allocator to generate feasible inputs to the ship.
The results showed
good performance in the ship simulator for almost all controllers. A combined controller consisting of
constant bearing, line-of-sight and integrator backstepping was proposed as a solution to the in-port
ferry manoeuvring problem.
==PAGE:4==

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Figure 1.1: The Scandlines hybrid ferries M/F Copenhagen and M/F Berlin in
the port of Rostock. Photo by Scandlines.
Scandlines operates a passenger route between Gedser in Denmark and Rostock in Germany. To this
end, the ferries M/F Copenhagen and M/F Berlin are used. These can be seen in Figure 1.1. The
Gedser-Rostock ferries are single-ended ferries and must therefore perform a 180-degree turn after
leaving the harbour in Gedser. Currently the manoeuvre is performed manually by the crew on board;
an illustration of how it’s typically performed can be seen in Figure 1.2.
An autonomous system for in-port ferry manoeuvring has several use-cases.
From an economic
perspective, automating the manoeuvring could optimise the ship steering resulting in reduced fuel
consumption or faster transits. Another use is the training of new navigators and captains. Here one
could imagine the system being used as a decision support to guide the trainees when unsure of how to
proceed in a given situation. A third use-case could be in the design of new harbours. Multiple ships
and harbour configurations could be run through simulations to generate various routes and figure out
an optimal harbour design.
With all this in mind, the purpose of this project is to create a guidance and control system that will
enable M/F Copenhagen to perform a turning manoeuvre out of Gedser harbour autonomously. The
guidance system will generate a route that the control system needs to follow, which enables a digital
twin of M/F Copenhagen to perform the manoeuvre.
Ship dynamics are in general highly nonlinear and complex. Therefore, model identification of the
nonlinear dynamics will be done using data-driven estimation methods to achieve a suﬀiciently simple
model of the ship dynamics. The estimated model will be used for the development of the autonomous
control system and will be based on real sensor data from M/F Berlin provided by FORCE Technology
and Scandlines. As testing on the real ship is idealistic, the designed control system will be evaluated
using an advanced ship simulator provided by FORCE Technology, i.e. the digital twin.
==PAGE:9==
1.1 Scope
2
Figure 1.2: Illustration of departure manoeuvre for The Gedser-Rostock ferries
leaving Gedser.
1.1
Scope
The project is concerned with developing a guidance and control system for autonomous departures of
M/F Copenhagen from Gedser Harbour. Only departures of M/F Copenhagen from Gedser Harbour
are considered; arrivals, other ships and other harbours are outside the project’s scope. However, a
system capable of departing M/F Copenhagen is assumed capable of performing the first part of an
arrival at Gedser, i.e. manoeuvring the ship to be in proximity of the dock. The main objective of the
project is to successfully depart M/F Copenhagen. Other factors such as economic considerations, e.g.
power consumption, will be secondary and only considered if the main objective is achieved.
The objective of the system identification is to develop and identify a mathematical model that can
accurately describe the dynamics M/F Copenhagen while still being suﬀiciently simple enough to be
used and analysed in control applications. As such, highly complex models derived from for example
neural networks and computational fluid dynamics (CFD) will not be considered. Rather, the system
identification will be limited to classical data-driven estimation methods.
The guidance system must be capable of generating a reference signal that is appropriate for the
control designs to track. Moreover, tracking the reference must be feasible without colliding with any
obstacles, particularly the harbour itself and the borders of the turning basin. To this end, topics from
rapidly-exploring random trees and dynamics optimisation will be applied.
For the control system, control strategies that are suitable for regulation of marine vessels where
exact measurements are available will be considered. This involves control systems where ship ma-
noeuvring, physical limitations of the ship and its actuators, and external forces present at sea can be
handled properly. For this purpose, tools from nonlinear control theory will be used in the development
of several control strategies.

Figure 1.3: An overview of the thesis structure along with an outline of the proposed solution to the
in-port ferry manoeuvring problem. Full lines indicate online work and dashed lines indicate offline
work.
• Chapter 2: Methodology
– A general introduction to the methodologies considered in this thesis.
• Chapter 3: System Description
– A thorough description of the system consisting of the ship and the harbour in Gedser.
Several ship particulars are listed, relevant assumptions are made and the kinematics and
kinetics are derived and discussed. Finally, a model of the harbour, turning basin and ship
outline is presented.
• Chapter 4: Data Processing and Analysis
– Data analysis and data processing done on the transit data supplied by FORCE Technology
and Scandlines. It also gives an overview of the wind characteristics around the harbour in
Gedser, which will be used for the testing of the control system.
• Chapter 5: System Identification
– Identification of the mathematical model of the ship described in Chapter 3. Theory regard-
ing relevant topics in system identification is introduced, which is then used to estimate the
model. The model is then assessed, and the use and limitations are discussed.
• Chapter 6: Guidance System
– Presents a guidance system, which uses the model estimated in chapter 5 and the weather
data discussed in chapter 4. Topics within Rapidly-Exploring Random Trees (RRTs) and
dynamical optimisation are presented. The methods are applied in a cascade such that the
result of one is fed into the other to create a reference for the control system.
==PAGE:11==
1.3 Sustainability
4
• Chapter 7: Virtual Inputs and Control Allocation
– The first of the two pillars of the control system: control allocation.
Different methods
are discussed to address the problem of distributing actuator commands to fulfil a desired
actuation on the system. The actuator commands are then passed to the ship / ship simulator
in closed-loop. Finally, a comparison of the presented methods and a discussion of their
limitations are given.
• Chapter 8: Control System
– The second of the two pillars of the control system: the control law. Here different methods
of controlling the ship to follow a reference, supplied by the guidance system, is considered.
The control law produces virtual inputs, which are then passed on to the control allocator.
• Chapter 9: Future Work
– A short overview of the different directions that could be explored as an extension of this
thesis.
1.3
Sustainability
As technology in general needs to be sustainable, engineers have a societal and environmental respon-
sibility to analyse the effects and possible ramifications of the technology they produce. Automating
ships is inherently a political question as it can lead to apparent reduced employment or more generally,
a need to up-skill the workforce. This is caused by automation displacing people in positions with
automatable tasks. These people would need to find other jobs or up-skill themselves to obtain jobs
with less automatable tasks. Captains might do less navigation and be hired to be available in the case
of unexpected events. Captains would be enabled to do other things while the ship would sail itself.
As the technology matured, one could imagine a shift towards centralised control facilities for a fleet of
ships. Instead of the captains having to find different employment, they would instead handle multiple
autonomous ships from land. Here they could intervene whenever an autonomous ship would run into
a situations it could not handle itself. This coincides with the notion of automation transforming jobs
[Nun21].
One could also consider the impact on the environment. Autonomous in-port ferry manoeuvring
has the potential to be an optimal control strategy, which could reduce the energy expenditure when
operating the ship. The automation of the manoeuvre could also reduce the number of errors made as a
computer does not get tired or distracted. Hence this thesis is a small contribution towards achieving the
Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). The goals and the specific targets that this thesis contributes
towards are given below.
Figure 1.4: Sustainable Development Goals.
• Goal 7: Ensure access to affordable, reliable, sustainable and modern energy for all
– Target 7.3: By 2030, double the global rate of improvement in energy eﬀiciency

• Goal 8: Promote sustained, inclusive and sustainable economic growth, full and productive
employment and decent work for all
– Target 8.2: Achieve higher levels of economic productivity through diversification, techno-

intensive sectors
• Goal 12: Ensure sustainable consumption and production patterns

1.4
Literature Review
This section presents an overview of the literature surrounding this thesis. It is divided into multiple
sections that cover each relevant topic. It starts out with literature related to system identification.
Then it presents literature related to guidance and then ends with control related literature.
1.4.1
System Identification
This section gives an overview of some of the literature related to identification of nonlinear systems
that was read as a part of this thesis.
Traditionally, identifying a dynamical model of a ship is done using to-scale model testing of said
ship and CFD (computational fluid dynamics) simulations [Bir19]. The to-scale ship model testing is
often performed in test facilities consisting of long water basins called towing tanks or circulating water
tunnels. These are equipped with wave makers for generating wave forces and sensors for collecting
data. Doing experiments in this smaller scaled environment then reflects the real ships behaviour, but
considerations needs to be taken in regard to the scaling to get proper results. As done in [CIV00; XS19],
the data collected from ship model tests can then be used to analyse and define dynamic characteristics
of the ship and a dynamical model of the ship can be achieved.
If such test facilities are not available CFD simulations may be used. Here a digital model of the ship
and waters are used. This digital model is based of geometrical and physical aspects of the ship and
surrounding waters. Simulating the ship this way, one can then obtain data which then can be used to
estimate a dynamical model of the ship, as done in [Ara+12]. Care needs to be taken when setting up
the simulation environment in order to acquire suﬀicient precision in the simulations. CFD is also very
computationally heavy and time consuming, but the increase in computational speed strongly favours
the progression of CFD simulations for use in ship model estimation [Bir19].
Testing facilities with towing tanks and a ship model of the M/S Copenhagen are not available
and using CFD simulations is out of the scope of this project.
Luckily, these methods are mainly
used for collecting data for which data-driven model estimation methods can be used to obtain a
dynamical model. As data from the ferry transits are available, similar model estimation methods can
be used to obtain a dynamical model of the M/F Copenhagen. [YY15] uses a least square method
(LSM) to determine hydrodynamic force coeﬀicients related to the hull, rudder and propeller based
on simulation data of a tanker. Similarly, [CIV00] uses least squares to estimate parameters based on
on-board sensor data from an UUV (Unmanned Underwater Vehicle) and [Ara+12] uses least squares
to estimate hydrodynamic and rudder coeﬀicients from CFD simulations. [Che+22] and [XS19] both
use least square support vector machine (LS-SVM) to identify parameters in a nonlinear dynamical
model of a ship. [Che+22] uses data from numerical simulations, while [XS19] uses data from towing
tank experiments.
A method which is more directly related to dynamical model estimation is Koopman theory. Modern
Koopman theory is based on the concept of which nonlinear dynamics are represented as an infinite
dimensional linear operator that represents all possible measurement functions. Using dynamic mode
==PAGE:13==
1.4 Literature Review
6
decomposition a discrete time approximation to the linear operator can be found. This further enables
the use of linear control design, which is well developed [Bru+21].
Another approach to dynamical system identification is Sparse Identification of Nonlinear Dynamics
(SINDy), which is a method to estimate parsimonious systems of ordinary differential equations [BPK16;
BPK]. Several extensions to SINDy exists. [Rud+16] introduces SINDy for partial differential equations.
[Fas+21] gives an overview of SINDy with the use of control signals in the dynamics.
The SINDy algorithm is sensitive to noise when trying to identify dynamics.
[KBN22] introduces
method to simultaneously denoise the data, estimate the noise probability distribution and estimate
sparse system dynamics. [KKB20] proposes a method to robustly identify implicit differential and partial
differential equations from noisy data. To account for abrupt changes in model dynamics, [Qua+18]
developed an adaptive method for updating a previously identified model with few data samples. [MJ23]
proposes a method to estimate models with a guarantee of satisfying side information, e.g. symmetries
and physical laws.
As SINDy has many extensions it has also been used in many different applications. Some of them are
thermodynamics, fluid dynamics and chemical processes as seen in [RS20; GBN21].
1.4.2
Guidance
There are many different ways of creating a route. [ZPD20] gives a survey and taxonomy of ship weather
routing in general. Here several approaches are presented in which to compute a route depending on
weather considerations. Some of the methods are based on genetic algorithms, dynamic programming,
simulation, nonlinear optimisation and sampling based techniques. [LM95] presents a dynamic pro-
gramming approach to minimise fuel consumption of transatlantic shipping in the presence of known
ocean currents. They do this by two complexity-reducing heuristics based on feasible regions dictated
by heading, time constraints and current data. These methods were later extended in [LM98] to a
stochastic and adaptive method where the ocean currents are not known but assumed to follow some
stochastic distribution based on measurements.
[FVH18] presents a weather routing system for choosing Pareto eﬀicient routes depending on factors
such as wind and current resistance, risk and travel time.
The routing system is able to identify
routes with navigationally safer waterways defined by the meteorological and oceanographic conditions
[FVH18].
[Buc+19; Buc+21] considers whole-body route planning for a multi-legged robot in a constrained
space. Here collision detection is done in each iteration to avoid the body of the robot colliding with
the space around it. A Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree (RRT) algorithm [LaV98] is used to compute
the movement of the robot from a current pose to a goal pose.
Many improvements and additions have been made to the original RRT algorithm. One is mentioned
in the original paper by LaValle [LaV98], which consists of goal biasing. Here the idea is to change
the sampling scheme to be biased towards sample points close to the goal state. This would bias the
growth of the tree towards the goal state and hopefully increase the probability of finding a feasible
route. Another variant is generating two trees with one root in the initial state, and another root in
the goal state. This is known as the RRT-Connect algorithm, which aggressively tries to connect the
two trees through a greedy heuristic [KL00]. The performance was found to be close to and often times
better than the ordinary RRT algorithm for several problems [KL00].
[KF10] introduced the Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree* (RRT*) algorithm that does refinement on
the tree in each iteration. [KF11] shows that the RRT* algorithm is asymptotically optimal.
In a similar fashion to the RRT algorithm, several additions to the RRT* algorithm exists. One of
them is the Informed RRT* algorithm used on problems seeking to minimise the route length [GSB14].
This algorithm constraints the sampling space to a prolate hyperspheroid when a feasible solution is
found. [GSB14] proved that if this subset is not sampled in the RRT* algorithm, the probability of
improving the solution in large worlds or high state dimensions become arbitrarily small [GSB14]. They
also tested the algorithm on several problems showing that the rate of convergence and final solution
cost of the Informed RRT* outperformed the RRT* algorithm.
==PAGE:14==
1.4 Literature Review
7
In addition to Informed RRT*, algorithms for use in autonomous marine crafts have also been
developed. [ERG21] has developed an algorithm that samples annulus and half-annulus subsets when
in a potential collision situation. The subsets are created such that the generated route adheres to
the International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea (COLREGs). Some of the same authors
later extended the algorithm to include depth information from the electronic navigation chart and
probabilistic models using kernel density estimation describing seafarers’ best practices [EG21].
1.4.3
Control
Standard control of marine surface vessels are often divided into control of underactuated surface vessels
(USV) and control of fully actuated surface vessels [Fos21]. Control of fully actuated surface vessels
assumes that the vessel can produce force in all directions (surge, sway and yaw) independently. It
is most commonly used in dynamic positioning (DP), where the vessel moves at low speeds and the
constraints on velocity and position of the vessel are more strict. A survey of dynamic position control
methods can be found in [Sør11].
These include PID controllers, nonlinear controllers and hybrid
controllers.
Control of USVs most often utilise that the vessel can only be controlled using forward motion and
turning (surge and yaw), thus the heading of the ship is used to steer [LF14].
Additionally, within marine control it is also common to differentiate between the control objective. For
marine control common control objectives are point stabilisation, trajectory tracking, path following
and target tracking [Bre10]. A common method of path following for USVs is using line-of-sight (LOS)
guidance [FP06]. Many variations of LOS exist as it is widely spread. For example, [BPP08] introduce
Integral LOS (ILOS) for straight paths which uses integral action to estimate a sideslip angle and
[ZSX18] introduce Error-Constrained LOS path following which ensures the vessel will stay within a
predefined error margin of the path at all times. A method used for target tracking is constant bearing
(CB) guidance [Bre10], where the goal of the vessel is to rendezvous with a target. This method was
first used in missile guidance, but is also applicable for marine vessels. CB guidance both works for
USVs as done in [BHF08] and for fully actuated surface vessels as in [BF05]. CB and LOS guidance
may also be combined as done in [BSF06] to achieve target tracking along a predefined path.
Non-bijective input mappings challenge most control designs. [Fos21] gives an overview of allocation
strategies for both fully and underactuated systems. Moreover, [JFB04] presents an allocation strategy
for ships with azipod thrusters that avoids configurations where the ability to produce force in any
direction is lost. [Han+11] incorporates elements from model predictive control to control allocation,
presenting a strategy that accounts for actuator dynamics, e.g. their response time to a given command.
[Fos21] suggests an integrator backstepping design applicable to fully actuated ships for low-speed
tracking in both position and heading. This design takes advantage of system properties to improve
robustness compared to feedback linearisation techniques. For underactuated ships, [Fos21] presents
a line-of-sight guidance system with an integrator backstepping velocity controller that guarantees
convergence in position and linear velocity.
Any complex system will inevitably have unmodelled dynamics. [Fos21] gives an overview of sliding-
mode strategies for robust control.
In addition, [Fos21] provides a sliding-mode heading autopilot
applicable to both ships and autonomous underwater vehicles. [Kim+15] applies an integral sliding
mode technique to stabilise the motion of an autonomous underwater vehicle with almost immediate
disturbance rejection. Their design achieves better performance when compared to both a feedback
linearised PD and an ordinary sliding mode controller. [Man+21] extends this strategy, proposing a
super-twisting integral sliding-mode design that also attenuates chatter.
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2
Methodology
This section consists of a short presentation of the different methodologies that are used in the thesis.
First, a short list of common terminology will be introduced. Then an explanation of data-driven model
estimation along with the methods used for this purpose. Following is an introduction to the methods
used in the guidance system, which consists of both offline and online guidance systems. Finally the
different control architectures used in the control system are explained.
2.1
Common Terminology
Several definitions are consistently used throughout the thesis. These will be mentioned here.
• Waypoint: A waypoint is defined as a point, (x, y), in the plane giving the position of an object.
• Pose: A pose is defined as a waypoint, (x, y), along with a heading, ψ.
• Path: A path is a collection of consecutive waypoints, (x, y), that may or may not be time-
dependent.
• Route: A route is a collection of consecutive poses, (x, y, ψ), that may or may not be time-
dependent.
• Reference: A reference is a C2 function that admits a pose for each time instance in the simulation
horizon.
This shows that a pose is an extension of a waypoint and that a route is an extension of a path.
Similarly, a reference is a continuous extension of a route.
2.2
Data-driven Model Estimation
Data-driven model estimation is the methodology of using statistical analysis and tools for estimating
parameters in a model that can describe a system given data of measurements/observations of said
system. This section introduces the two main methods used for model estimation of the ferry in the
project.
2.2.1
Support Vector Regression
Support Vector Regression (SVR) stems from the theory of Support Vector Classification, both utilising
Support Vector Machines. In classical SVM classification, the goal is to find a nonlinear (or linear)
optimal separating hyperplane between two nonseparable classes [HTF08]. Given a set of data points,
the separating hyperplane is constructed by transforming the feature space into a higher dimensional
feature space and then fitting a linear optimal separating hyperplane in this transformed space using
support vectors. As such, when projected onto the original feature space the hyperplane will perceived
as nonlinear and can then more easily separate the two classes than a linear hyperplane could. The
support vectors here give the supporting data points that help shape and define the hyperplane. The
==PAGE:16==
2.3 Guidance Systems
9
transformed feature space is most often defined by a kernel function given by the inner product of a
transformation/basis function of the features. One can then utilise the kernel-trick [HTF08], such that
only an explicit expression for the kernel is needed and not the implicit transformation function. This
can greatly simplify the computations needed for making predictions. Different kernel functions then
also shape the hyperplane differently.
These principles for SVM classification can then be adapted for regression. Here, instead of fitting
a separating hyperplane, the problem consists of finding a function that solves the regression problem
for a given set of observations and features.
2.2.2
Sparse Identification of Nonlinear Dynamical systems
Sparse Identification of Nonlinear Dynamical systems (SINDy) is an estimation method used for data-
driven model estimation of dynamical systems. The inherent assumption in SINDy is that the governing
equations defining the dynamical system are sparse in a high-dimensional nonlinear function space
[BPK16]. Hence, it leverages that only a few relevant terms define the dynamics of the system. By
applying knowledge of the system, physical laws, etc., it is then possible to setup a library of candidate
nonlinear functions that make up the space of possible functions defining the governing equation. These
candidate functions can be functions of the states, control inputs and other features. Using sparse
regression methods, one can then determine the sparse set of functions in the library of candidate
nonlinear functions that define the dynamics of the system.
2.3
Guidance Systems
This section introduces different guidance laws that will be used in the project. These include guid-
ance laws used in online guided motion control and guidance laws used for offline reference trajectory
generation.
2.3.1
Constant Bearing Guidance
Constant Bearing (CB) guidance is a two-point guidance scheme involving a target point and interceptor
point, see Figure 2.1. The goal is for the interceptor to converge to the target by aligning the relative
Figure 2.1: Constant bearing guidance and its relation to parallel navigation [Bre10].
interceptor-target velocity along the line of sight between the interceptor and the target [Bre10]. CB
guidance works by making the rotation rate of the line of sight go to zero such that the interceptor
perceives the target at a constant bearing as shown in the figure. Here, the line-of-sight is the direct
path between the interceptor and the target. The CB guidance scheme is often referred to as parallel
navigation (PN) due to the line-of-sight vectors moving in parallel to each other. The CB guidance
method is most commonly used for air-to-air missiles, where an interceptor needs to collide with a moving
target, also known as target tracking. In this project, CB guidance will be implemented through direct
velocity assignment as done in [BHF08], see Figure 2.2 It will be used at a kinematic level in order to
==PAGE:17==
2.3 Guidance Systems
10
compute velocity references that will be fed to a kinetic controller in order for the ship to converge to
the ideal ship position and velocity on a predefined route.
Figure 2.2: Constant bearing using direct velocity assignment [BHF08].
Even though it involves guidance principles, CB will be described in detail in the Control chapter,
Chapter 8, not the Guidance chapter, Chapter 6, as it will be used as part of a guided motion control
architecture.
2.3.2
Line-Of-Sight Guidance
Line-of-sight (LOS) guidance is a three-point guidance scheme involving a reference point, target point
and interceptor point, see Figure 2.3. The goal is for the interceptor to converge to the target by moving
Figure 2.3: Line-of-sight guidance visualisation [Fos21].
along the LOS vector between the reference and target [Fos21]. In this project LOS guidance will be
used on a kinematic level in order to compute velocity references that will be fed to a kinetic controller
in order for the ship to converge to a predefined path. This is also known as path following.
Even though it involves guidance principles, LOS will be described in detail in the Control chapter,
Chapter 8, not the Guidance chapter, Chapter 6, as it will be used as part of a guided motion control
architecture.
==PAGE:18==
2.4 Control
11
2.3.3
Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree
Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree (RRT) is a method that rapidly explores the collision free state space.
It does this by uniformly sampling points in the collision free state space, which it then tries to grow
the tree towards. The method does iterative collision detection and validation of model dynamics such
that any path throughout the tree is valid. The method itself does not generate routes but the tree can
be used to generate routes between different nodes in the tree that satisfies certain criteria. This thesis
will consider the criteria of the shortest weighted distance between nodes through the tree.
2.3.4
Dynamic Optimisation
Dynamic optimisation incorporates system dynamics into optimisation problems. To elaborate, consider
the discrete system
xk = f (xk−1, uk) ,
(2.1)
where xk ∈Rn are the states and uk ∈Rm are the inputs. A corresponding dynamic optimisation
problem may be expressed as
min
x1,...,xK,
u1,...,uK
ρ(xK) +
K
X
k=1
ℓ(xk, uk),
(2.2)
s.t.
xk = f(xk−1, uk),
for k = 1, . . . , K
(2.3)
g(xk, uk) ≤gub
for k = 1, . . . , K,
(2.4)
h(xk, uk) = hub
for k = 1, . . . , K,
(2.5)
Here, (2.3) enforces the discrete dynamics, while (2.4)-(2.5) enforce inequality and equality constraints,
respectively. The above formulation assumes an initial condition, x0, is provided. The objective consists
of terminal, ρ(xK), and running, ℓ(xk, uk), cost functions.
These are usually defined to penalise
undesirable behaviour and input actuation, and each should have a lower bound for the problem to be
well-defined. Many eﬀicient solvers are gradient-based, thus requiring differentiability of the objective,
system dynamics and all constraints. The result of a dynamic optimisation problem is an optimal input
sequence, u∗
1 . . . u∗
K, that induces the desired behaviour of the states.
This thesis will use dynamic optimisation for offline guidance, allowing the identified in-port dynam-
ics to be accounted for when generating routes.
2.4
Control
This section will consider the concept of virtual inputs, control allocation and different control strategies
for use in the motion control of the ship.
2.4.1
Virtual Inputs and Control Allocation
Consider the problem of stabilising the nonlinear system
˙x = fx(x) + h(u),
(2.6)
where x ∈Rn are the states and u ∈Rm are the inputs. Designing a suitable control law can be
challenging, particularly if h(·) is not a bijective mapping, in which case a well-defined inverse does
not exist. To ease the design process, one can substitute h(·) with virtual inputs, τctrl ∈Rn, thereby
simplifying (2.6) to
˙x = fx(x) + τctrl.
(2.7)
A control law can then be designed for (2.7) using τctrl as if they were arbitrary inputs.
==PAGE:19==
2.4 Control
12
Although they simplify the design process, virtual inputs cannot be applied to the system (2.6).
Given τctrl from the control law, the actual inputs, u, must be distributed such that
τctrl = h (u) .
This is referred to as control allocation, and u is said to be allocated, to produce the desired virtual
input.
2.4.2
Motion Control Systems
This section presents the different motion control systems considered in this project. Many different
controllers are used and all of these assumes a model of the ship.
2.4.2.1
Feedback Linearisation
Designing a suitable control law can be simplified by linearising the system using the input. This allows
the use of linear control theory and simplifies other nonlinear designs. Input-to-state linearisation is one
such technique; it transforms the system into a so-called controllable canonical form [EL91], afterwards
all nonlinearities are cancelled.
2.4.2.2
Full-State Feedback and Linear Quadratic Regulation
Full-state feedback is a control strategy that utilises linear functions of the states to guarantee uniform
global exponential stability of controllable linear systems [HJS08]. There are various techniques within
linear theory to determine suitable input functions. Linear Quadratic Regulation (LQR) [HJS08] finds
optimal input functions by solving a quadratic optimisation problem.
2.4.2.3
Integrator Backstepping
Integrator backstepping is a feedback stabilisation technique that is applicable to systems in strict-
feedback form [Kha02], e.g. the system
˙x1 = f1 (x1) + g1 (x1) x2,
(2.8)
˙x2 = f2 (x1, x2) + g2 (x1, x2) u,
(2.9)
where the solution of (2.9) can be considered as an input to (2.8). Compared to input-to-state lineari-
sation, integrator backstepping has the potential to relax the assumption made on the system, thereby
increasing the design’s robustness. In particular, it renders stabilising x1 a tracking problem in x2,
allowing gain matrices to be defined in x2 coordinate basis. The analysis in this thesis will be limited
to systems on the form (2.8)-(2.9). [Kha02] extends integrator backstepping to an arbitrary number of
states, x1 . . . xn.
2.4.2.4
Sliding Mode Control
Sliding mode control is a discontinuous control strategy that is robust to unmodelled but bounded
disturbances. Robustness is achieved by forcing the trajectories onto a sliding surface by dominating
the disturbances. On the sliding surface, the disturbances do not affect the system, and the trajectories
behave in some predefined fashion, usually converging to zero at an exponential rate.
2.4.2.5
Model Predictive Control
Model predictive control is a control strategy based on minimisation of an objective function with
respect to a control signal and states.
System dynamics are usually included as constraints in the
optimisation problem or by taking advantage of model structure such that it can be included in the
==PAGE:20==
2.4 Control
13
objective function. Constraints can also be used to model actuator saturation or saturation in the
actuator derivative. An essential concept in model predictive control is the prediction horizon. The
prediction horizon is the time frame for which the control system is predicted into the future based on
a control sequence. The control sequence is chosen as to minimise the objective function subject to any
constraints.
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 3
System Description
In this chapter a mathematical description of the system will be derived. For this purpose, the ship
itself along with its dynamics and a model of the Gedser Harbour and turning basin will be considered.
First, a general description of the ship is given. Then, the reference frames used in the modelling are
explained. Later, the assumptions of the models are presented along with the model itself. Finally, an
explanation of the harbour and turning basin models are described along with a description of the ship
outline model used by the control system.
3.1
General Ship Description
The Gedser-Rostock ferries consist of the two ships: M/F Copenhagen and M/F Berlin. This section
will consider M/F Copenhagen. In service condition M/F Copenhagen is 169.5 m long and 24.8 m wide.
The ship has 1 variable-pitch centre propeller paired with a rudder and 2 fixed-pitch azipod thrusters
located at the aft of the ship. The azipod thrusters are able to generate propulsion in a large range
of directions. To avoid the flow wash working against the ship, limitations have been put in place for
the angles that the azipod thrusters can attain. The limitations are 60◦to 150◦towards the hull, i.e.
positive values for the starboard unit and negative values for the port unit [ES16]. In the front of the
ship there are 2 bow thrusters, which are used for transversal propulsion. The propeller, rudder and
thruster locations can be seen illustrated in Figure 3.1 [ES16]. The particulars of the ship can be seen
in Table 3.1 [ES16].

3.2
Assumptions
As with all real systems, some simplifying assumptions need to be made. These are listed below.
• Relatively Calm Weather
The weather, e.g. wind and waves, will be assumed to be relatively calm, i.e. upper bounded by
the 0.9-quantile of observed values at Gedser Harbour.
• Rigid-Hull
The hull of the ship is assumed rigid.
As the ship will only be considered in relatively calm
weather, the effect of the ship flexing is deemed negligible.
• Homogeneous Mass Distribution and Symmetry
The ship is assumed to have a homogeneous mass distribution and a symmetry in the xz-plane.
Hence Ixy = Iyz = 0, where these are cross inertia terms.
• Inertial NED frame
The North-East-Down frame as described below is assumed inertial. As the ship will only be
considered in the Gedser Harbour and turning basin in a limited time frame, the effect of the
rotation of the earth is negligible.
• 3 Degrees of Freedom
As the ship will only be considered in relatively calm weather, it is assumed that roll, pitch and
heave are negligible in a control perspective. Hence the model will have 3 Degrees of Freedom
(DOF): surge, sway and yaw.
==PAGE:23==
3.3 Kinematics
16
• Irrotational Ocean Currents
The ocean currents will be assumed to be irrotational, i.e. they have no angular velocity, Vc =
[uc, vc, 0]T , and its velocity components are constant.
• Unknown Wave Forces
Wave forces will not be modelled and are hence assumed unknown.
• Waterline Ship Size
The ship will be assumed to have no 3rd dimension and only consists of the extended waterline of
the ship. The extended waterline is the waterline of the ship in service condition that has been
extended in the aft direction as to resemble the true size of the ship [ES16]. It has also been
extended sideways as to create a buffer. This is described in Section 3.5.
• Constant Propeller Thrust and Torque Coeﬀicients
To make modelling the ship easier, the propeller thrust and torque coeﬀicients, KT , KQ, are
assumed to be constant.
• Fixed-Pitch Propeller
As no recorded sensor data about the pitch of the centre propeller has been supplied, the propeller
is assumed to be fixed pitch in the modelling of the ship.
3.3
Kinematics
To develop a mathematical description of the dynamics, the geometrical aspects of motion and the
forces causing the motion must be considered [Fos21]. The former is denoted kinematics and the latter
is denoted kinetics. To start out, a description of the kinematics will be provided.
(b)

y(b)
x(n)
(n)
y
Figure 3.2: An illustration of NED and BODY reference frames.
When analysing the motion of a marine craft in 3 DOFs, the position of the ship and the forces acting
on the ship need to be considered. The position needs to be described relative to an inertial reference
frame, while the forces acting on the ship are more readily modelled using a body-fixed reference frame.
A description of these reference frames can be seen below [Fos21]:
• NED: The North-East-Down coordinate frame denoted by {n} =
 x(n), y(n), z(n)

where
– x(n) - axis points towards true North,
– y(n) - axis points towards East,
==PAGE:24==
3.3 Kinematics
17
– z(n) - axis is positive downwards and normal to the Earth’s surface.
The origin of the reference frame, o(n), is defined in relation to Earth’s reference ellipsoid (World
Geodetic System 1984). This is given by a latitude µ0, a longitude l0, and a height h0. In this
project the origin is given by,
o(n) =
 µ0, l0, h0

h0 = 0 m.

This corresponds to a point approximately 250 meters south-west of Gedser Harbour. See Fig-
ure 3.3 for an illustration.
• BODY: The body-fixed reference frame denoted by {b} =
 x(b), y(b), z(b)

is a reference frame
where the axes move with the position and heading of the ship. Here,
– x(b) - longitudinal axis, which is positive from aft to fore,
– y(b) - transversal axis, which is positive towards starboard,
– z(b) - normal axis, which is positive downward.
The origin of the reference frame, o(b), is fixed to the body of the ship and hence moves with the
position of the craft. It is defined as being in the centreline of the ship and 0.5 · Loa = 84.75
meters from the aft of the ship outline. See Figure 3.1 and Figure 3.2 for illustrations.

3.4.1
Additional Hydrodynamic Forces
Several hydrodynamic effects that influence the dynamics of the ship are not included in the mathemat-
ical model above. As Gedser Harbour and surrounding areas can be classified as shallow and restricted
waterways, hydrodynamic effects related to this will here be presented. Specifically, the squat and bank
effects will be considered.
The squat effect is the change in a ship’s heave when in motion. It is usually described as a sinkage
of the ship’s hull where the amount of sinkage increases the less clearence under the keel there is and
the faster the ship sails. Depending on the size of the ship, a sizeable difference between the amount
of sinkage in the bow and stern of the ship can be seen [Gou11]. Though heave is not included in the
considered mathematical model, a dislocation in heave affect the dynamics of the ship in surge, sway
and yaw and the effectiveness of thrusters [Tan+20].
1atan2 https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atan2
2Constant due to the assumptions.
==PAGE:28==
3.5 Model of Harbour, Turning Basin and Ship Outline
21
The bank effect is the effect of the stern of the ship being swung towards a bank or another ship
when in motion. The bank effect was somewhat recently demonstrated when the large container ship
Ever Given ran aground in the Suez Canal for six days. This was said to be in part caused by bank
effects [YG21].
The squat and bank effects are caused by the water being displaced by the ship in motion. If the
ship is in a shallow and restricted waterway, the displaced water will be squeezed under and along
its hull, which will create an increase in speed of the flow as water is approximately incompressible.
This induces a pressure decrease, which creates suction forces and moments [LVD18]. These forces and
moments are the squat and bank effects.
Experiments show that the squat and bank effects are approximately proportional to the speed
squared and inversely proportional to the underwater keel clearance and distance to bank, respectively
[Gou11; LVD18].
3.5
Model of Harbour, Turning Basin and Ship Outline
Models of Gedser Harbour, the turning basin outside Gedser Harbour and the outline of the ship are
needed for use in the guidance and control systems and for general simulations. For ease of communica-
tion both the harbour and turning basin will be referred to as just the harbour. Two different models of
the harbour will be considered - one detailed and one simplified. Only the detailed model will be denoted
as such. The models were created using a navigational chart of the harbour and the surrounding area.
The model represents an area with a depth of 6 meters or more. This is in correspondence with the
draught of the ship being 5.46 meters [ES16]. The model of the outline of the ship was created from
simplifying data of the waterline of the ship in service condition. The ship outline model was lengthened
as to avoid the azipod thrusters being outside of the ship outline. The ship outline model is 170 meters
in length and 25 meters in breadth whereas the real ship has a length of Loa = 169.5 meters and a
breadth of B = 24.8 meters. The ship outline relative to the harbour can be seen in Figure 3.4(a).
The detailed model represents the harbour and a longer stretch of the fairway. This can be seen in
Figure 3.3. Similarly, the simplified harbour outline can be seen in Figure 3.4(a).
From the created simplified outline of the Gedser harbour and the outline of the ship, polygons are
created consisting of the vertices and edges of the outlines. The polygon of the Gedser harbour can be
seen in Figure 3.4(b). The coordinates are shown in the NED reference frame. Note that the polygon
itself is the blue-shaded area. The polygon of the ship is not shown as it is simply the interior of the
ship outline seen in Figure 3.4(a).

CHAPTER 4
Data Processing and
Analysis
For doing data-driven modelling of any kind, one does indeed need data. The following sections will
cover a description of the data provided for this project and an analysis, processing and synthesis of
said data to suit the needs for the system modelling and identification part of this project.
The data was provided by DTU and FORCE Technology in corporation with Scandlines. The data
originates from Task 1.4 of the ShippingLab project (2019-2021) [Shi19], which was done in coorporation
with DTU, FORCE Technology and Scandlines.
The provided data is collected on-board the ferry
M/F Berlin, sistership to M/F Copenhagen, on the Scandlines route Gedser-Rostock [Shi19]. A more
elaborate description of the data is provided in Section 4.1.
It is important to emphasise that the data provided and used in this project is not the raw data
collected from M/F Berlin for Task 1.4. As part of Task 1.4, the raw data had initially been collected
and processed by Rasmus Nielsen to be used in a machine learning environment. As such, the data
used in this project is a subset of the final and already processed data used for Task 1.4. Even though
the data has already been processed for a machine learning environment, it is still not entirely suitable
for this project’s purposes and will therefore need to be processed further. This is, among other, due to
some poor interpolation practices in the processing prior to this project. More on this in Section 4.2.
4.1
Data Description
The data used in this project stems from logs collected on-board the ferry M/F Berlin and consists of 54
different transits of about 2 hours each, all collected between the 7th of October 2019 and the 23rd of
October 2019 [Shi19]. The data consist of multiple Gedser-Rostock transits and Rostock-Gedser transits.
The data are split according to transit and stored in separate .dat files following the date/time naming
scheme transit_mm_dd_yyyy_hhmmss_cmnd.dat, e.g. the file transit_10_07_2019_085536_cmnd.dat
consists of data for the transit which started on the 7th of October 2019 at 08:55:36. The number of
time samples in each file ranges from 3125 to 3817.
Each row in the data files represents a time sample and the given data is sampled every 2 seconds.
Each column represents a variable that has either been measured on the ferry or has been created in
the original processing of the data. A total of 32 to 33 different variables are available at each time
sample. One variable was not consistently present throughout the data files and was therefore ignored.
This variable was not of relevance for the project and could therefore easily be deleted. A description of
the variables present in the original unprocessed data are presented in [Shi19]. The naming convention
and a short description of the data variables given and used in this project can be seen in Table 4.1.
These include the pose, velocity, propulsion system inputs of the ferry along with wind and current
variables. A time series of the data variables for one of the Gedser→Rostock transits can also be seen
in Figure 4.1. Here it can be seen that almost all variables are slowly varying when at open sea, while
in and close to harbour the variables are more varying. This can be explained by more manoeuvring
being needed when in the harbour due to banks, other ships, berthing, unberthing etc.. While at open
sea the conditions are more steady and the manoeuvre mostly involves sailing in a straight, forward
motion. The large jumps at the start and end of the transit in angular variables are due to jumps

The direction of the azipods and the RPM of the bow thrusters are given by the commanded input
and not the active status of the inputs. The status was included in the original data, but not in the
data provided for the project.
As such, it is assumed that the recorded commanded input is well
representative of the recorded status of the input at each time sample. Alternatively, one could apply
a filter to the commanded inputs to approximate the status of the input.
As mentioned, the provided data had already been processed beforehand.
Some features worth
mentioning are that the original raw data sets consist of recorded sensor signals which had uneven
sampling intervals. The time series of each signal therefore had to be re-sampled and aligned in order
to be collected in a common data frame, hence resulting in a common sampling time of 2 seconds. To
align and make sure data was available at each time sample, interpolation was applied to the signals,
but the interpolation was not applied properly taking into consideration the nature of the recorded
variable. This will be elaborated in the next section. Additionally, the original data were not split into
transits and to identify the transits it was decided that a transit starts when the speed over ground
exceeds 0.5 knots and the transits end when the speed over ground drops below 0.15 knots. This means
that no data are available for zero speed over ground and each transit starts when the ferry is already
in motion and ends when the ferry is still in motion [Shi19].
The manoeuvring of the ship and the time series of variables for transits ending in Rostock and
transits ending in Gedser differ significantly when not at open sea. As the main objective of this project
revolves around Gedser Harbour, these differences are worth investigating and taking into consideration.
A plot of some of the most notable variables describing the differences in the manoeuvre when leaving
and arriving in Gedser can be seen in Figure 4.2. These includes the position, heading and surge velocity
of the ferry. It can be seen that when leaving Gedser, the ferry reverses out of the harbour indicated by
the negative surge velocity. It then makes an approximately 170◦turn in the turning basin indicated
by the heading going from about 340◦to 170◦. After turning, the ship continues with a positive surge
velocity into the fairway. For transits arriving in Gedser it can be seen that the ship keeps a steady

The controls given by the centre propeller, rudder, azipods and bow thrusters can be examined
for respectively leaving and arriving in Gedser to get a better understanding of how these are used in
each manoeuvre. A plot of the centre propeller RPM, main rudder angle, starboard and port azipod
RPM and direction and the two bow thruster RPM for the two manoeuvres in Figure 4.2 can be seen
in Figure 4.3. Here it can be seen that, while close to harbour, the rudder and propeller are barely
used and only when a tighter turn of the ship is required the rudder may be used. They are mostly
used when the ferry begins to enter the fairway and accelerate forward into open waters where less
manoeuvrability is required. In harbour, primarily the azipods are used for manoeuvring. As explained
earlier the large jump in direction is due to small fluctuations between values close to −180◦and 180◦.
For leaving Gedser, it is seen that the azipods are turned 180◦, thus generating thrust for the ship to
reverse. When the 180-turn begins it can be seen that the azipods are turned to the starboard side,
thus applying a negative torque to the ship and turning it port side. Notice that when turning the
ship, the azipods are used separately, hence they have different RPMs and directions. Before and after
turning, the azipods are in sync, having the same RPM and direction. This is a setting the captain
==PAGE:33==
4.1 Data Description
26
Figure 4.2: Example difference in position, heading and surge velocity when leaving (left) Gedser and
arriving (right) at Gedser.
can enable. Similarly, the two bow thrusters are turned on with negative RPM in order to generate
additional negative torque for a sharper turn. The bow thrusters are almost always in sync, using the
same RPM. Once the ship has turned suﬀiciently, the bow thrusters are turned off and the azipods are
turned to 0◦to produce primarily forward thrust.
For transits arriving at Gedser, the azipods are used separately. This is because having the azipods
off-sync enables higher manoeuvrability, which is desired while berthing. It can be seen that the port
azipod is mainly used to brake the ship, having direction 180◦, while the starboard azipod is mainly
used to steer, having more varying directions but still directed forward. Additionally, the bow thrusters
are used to produce positive torque, turning the ship in towards the dock at the end.
The descriptions and plots of the manoeuvres given here generalise well to other transits. Some varia-
tions are to be expected between transits due to different weather, currents, which captain was sailing
that day, etc..

4.2
Data Processing and Synthesis
The provided data is split into separate files for each transit, and it can therefore be troublesome doing
any analysis, processing and modelling. Therefore, the data files are concatenated into one combined
file. This yields a total of 188,475 data samples covering the 54 transits. To then differ between transits,
an indicator is created denoting which transit a sample belongs to. This indicator is numbered from 1
to 54, e.g. all data samples from the first transit file transit_10_07_2019_085536_cmnd.dat gets the
transit indicator 1. Henceforth, specific transits will be referred to by their transit indicator, i.e. transit
1-54.
Similarly, to differ between outgoing transits of Gedser and ingoing transits of Gedser, a binary indicator
is created at each sample, i.e. 1 for outgoing transits and 0 for ingoing transits. This indicator is created
by exploiting that all outgoing transits will start with a negative surge velocity, while all ingoing transits
will start with a positive surge velocity.
As the attentive reader may have noticed, in Table 4.1 both the Lat and Long variables have two
==PAGE:35==
4.2 Data Processing and Synthesis
28
different units with thereto ranges, decimal degree and degree-minutes. This is due to the last 20 out of
the 54 transits having the Lat and Long variables stored in a deg-min format, while the first 34 transits
have them stored in a decimal degree format. This was not considered in the data processing prior to
this project and the Lat and Long variables therefore had to be transformed to coincide across the whole
data set. Here decimal degree was chosen, as it is simpler to work with in a modelling environment.
The deg-min format is stored such that the first two digits give the degrees while the remaining digits
give the minutes, i.e., 5419.57 corresponds to 54 degrees 19.57 minutes. Unfortunately, transits with
Lat and Long in degree-minutes had already been interpolated. As such, implausible data points occur
when there is a step change from 60 minutes to degrees and a point in between has been interpolated
without taking the nature of degree-minute data into consideration. An example of this can be seen
Table 4.2(a). Here it can be seen that due to not considering the data type, the interpolation results

in an implausible data point that deviates about 16 km from the two surrounding data points; 15 min
in longitude at around 54◦latitude corresponds to about 16 km. This is easily fixed by first removing
all such occurrences of poor interpolation, then converting the deg-min format to decimal degrees and
finally interpolating the removed samples in decimal degrees. Applying this procedure to the whole
dataset, gives the result in Table 4.2(b).
Now that all latitude and longitude data coincides properly, the latitude and longitude coordinates
can be transformed to the NED reference frame given in Section 3.3. The route between Gedser and
Rostock is fairly local, especially when the focus is on Gedser Harbour and the accompanying turning
basin. As such, the transformation to NED can safely be assumed to well approximate the position of
the ferry. This makes future modelling, analysis and implementation a lot less complicated.
Another occurrence where poor interpolation practices have had an impact is in the angular data.
Here it has not been taken into consideration that for angular data in range [−180◦, 180◦], the angles
−180◦and 180◦are the same.
Similarly for angular data in radians.
This means that when an
interpolation has taken place at a point between a sample with angle −180◦and a sample with angle
180◦, the resulting interpolated angle is poorly estimated to be somewhere in between. To illustrate
the effect of this, consider the blue lines in Figure 4.4 portraying the starboard azipod direction for
transit 34. The left plot shows the direction wrapped in range [−180◦, 180◦], which is how the data
is stored, while the right plot shows the direction unwrapped. From the left plot, no immediate error
can be spotted, but when examining the right plot it can be seen that there are large spikes deviating
from the expected direction. These spikes are exactly caused by interpolation in the wrapped space
instead of interpolation in the unwrapped space. This can potentially affect and result in an inaccurate
data-estimated model, as the data is not accurately representative of actual input. If one instead remove
the poorly interpolated directions, unwrap the angular data, and then interpolate the removed data
points, the red lines in Figure 4.4 are achieved. The red and blue lines are similar for the wrapped plot,
but the difference is much more apparent in the unwrapped plot. Interpolating in the unwrapped space
results in a much better representative time series of directions, which correctly estimates the direction

while taking into consideration the nature of the angular data. This procedure is then repeated for all
the angular data seen in Table 4.1 to ensure that the interpolated data samples accurately represent
the data.
Through inspection, a major flaw in the data was discovered. For transit 1 to 33 it was found that
the positional data, i.e., longitude and latitude, did not coincide with the rest of the data for the transit.
Hence, for transits classified as outgoing from Gedser, the positional data indicated that it was ingoing
and vice versa for ingoing transits. This is shown in Figure 4.5. Here, Figure 4.5(a) shows how actual
outgoing and ingoing transits should look like in latitude and surge velocity and Figure 4.5(b) shows
how the transits with identified wrong positional data look like in latitude and surge velocity. From
this it can be seen that for transit 1-33 the surge velocity correctly matches the other equally identified
transits. However, as transits leaving Gedser should naturally have a decreasing latitude down towards
Germany and opposite for transits arriving in Gedser, it can easily be seen that transits 1-33 have
positional data, which match the opposite direction. To identify that it was only the positional data
that was wrong, almost all data variables were cross-examined. It was concluded that only the latitude
and longitude were not matching the rest of the data. From the plots it seems that there has been
a mixup between the positional data from the transits when the original data set were constructed.
Though it was tried, it was not possible to identify which transits had been mixed around in order
to properly rearrange the positional data for their correct transit. This basically renders transit 1-33
useless for any purpose where positional data is used or needed, though it can still be used for purposes
not requiring positional data.
To potentially model hydrodynamic effects that stem from the hull of the ship interacting with
harbour banks, submerged banks, seabed banks, etc., additional measurements of the distances from
the ship to those banks may be necessary, see section 3.4.1. Luckily, such distances can be synthesised
by using the recorded pose of the ship given by N, E and Heading together with the model outline of the
ship and the model outline of Gedser Harbour and turning basin. Hence, given the pose of the ship and
a point on the ship, the distance to the harbour/turning basin can be computed. For this purpose, six
points on the ship are chosen: one at the fore, df, one at the aft, da, two points to the starboard side,
{dfs, das}, and two points to the port side, {dfp, dap}, respectively placed at the fore and aft. The six
distances can be seen illustrated in Figure 4.6. The six distances are only computed for transit 34-54,
as the positional data for transit 1-33 cannot be used. Additionally, these distances are only valid and
relevant within the harbour and turning basin outline, hence they are only computed for ship poses
that are contained therein.
Lastly, similar datatypes are converted to be of the same unit. Thus, converting all velocities and
speeds to m/s, all angular data to degrees in [−180◦, 180◦], and Turnrate to deg/s. This severely limits
==PAGE:37==

4.3 Wind Data Description
32
4.3
Wind Data Description
Data from the Danish Meteorological Institute (DMI) was collected [Dan24] to analyse the behaviour
of the wind around the harbour in Gedser. The data consisted of hourly averages of wind speed and
wind direction from 19:00 UTC 04/12-2013 until 13:00 UTC 17/01-2024, i.e. an approximate 10 year
data collection horizon. This corresponds to 87, 659 data samples. A characterisation of the wind speed
and direction can be seen on Figure 4.7(a) [Per24]. It is seen that the wind comes from the south to
south-west directions the most often. Particularly, the median wind direction is 217 degrees. In general,
wind speeds vary between 0 and 26.4 m/s. The empirical cumulative distribution function for the wind
speeds can be seen in Figure 4.7(b). Here it is seen that 90% of the wind speed observations are below
11 m/s. These results will be used in simulation scenarios.
(a) The wind speeds and directions for an approximate
10 year data collection horizon.

CHAPTER 5
System Identification
This chapter covers the identification of the mathematical model of the ship described in Chapter 3.
For this purpose, data-driven estimation methods will be applied using the data covered in Chapter 4.
The two main data-driven estimation methods used for this project are Support Vector Regression
(SVR) [Dru+97] and Sparse Identification of Nonlinear Dynamical systems (SINDy) [BPK16], which
are described in detail in Section 5.1 and Section 5.2, respectively. Figure 5.1 shows how these methods
are used in cascade; SVR will be used for identifying a baseline model of the ferry, while SINDy will
be used for estimating potential unmodelled dynamics not considered in the baseline model.
The
results of applying each data-driven estimation method will be given in their respective section, while
an assessment and presentation of the final model to be used in the control design will be given in
Section 5.3.

Regression
System Identification
SINDy

5.1
Support Vector Regression
This section first provides a general description of the theory of Support Vector Regression. Afterwards
a presentation of how SVR is applied in this project and the accompanying results are given.
5.1.1
Theory
Given a training sample set (xi, yi) for i = 1 . . . N, xi ∈Rm and yi ∈R, consider the regression problem
of finding a linear function f : Rm →R of the form
f(x) = βT x + b,
(5.1)
where β ∈Rm and b ∈R. SVR then seeks a function f which is as flat as possible, i.e, minimal ∥β∥2,
such that all errors are within some error margin ϵ > 0, i.e., |yi −f(xi)| < ϵ. That such a function exists
is not at all guaranteed, thus the slack variables ξi and ξ∗
i can be introduced to cope with infeasible
constraints. This leads to the linear SVR optimisation problem given by [SSS04]
minimize

5.1 Support Vector Regression

The constant C > 0 controls the trade-off between the penalty for observations outside the ϵ margin
and the flatness of f. As such it needs to be tuned in order to achieve the best model estimation. This
problem corresponds to the linear ϵ-sensitive loss function Vϵ [SSS04], which is given by
Lϵ =

(5.3)
The observations that are outside the ϵ-margin contribute to the loss, and thereby form the support
vectors. Hence the name support vector regression, as only these are used to regress on.
For problems where m > N, (5.2) can be computationally simpler to solve in its Lagrange dual
formulation [SSS04]. Using Lagrange multipliers, α, α∗∈RN, the dual optimisation problem is given
as [SSS04]

formulation.
Let the map φ : Rm →Rd project the data xi into some higher dimensional feature space, d ≥m.
Similarly to the linear case, the problem considered is finding a function such that
f(x) = βT φ(x) + b.

This means that it is only necessary to know the kernel K and not the mapping φ explicitly to formulate
f(x) and the optimisation problem (5.8). This is known as the kernel trick [HTF09]. This is desirable
as even for simple kernels K, the associated φ may be high dimensional and complex. Some common
kernel functions include:
Linear :
K(xi, xj) = xT
i xj
Gaussian :
K(xi, xj) = e−∥xi−xj∥2
Polynomial(q) :
K(xi, xj) = (1 + xT
i xj)q
(5.12)
If β needs to be explicitly known, then it is necessary to know φ. This is simple for the linear kernel,
but for the Gaussian and polynomial kernel φ is highly complex1,2.
5.1.2
Model and Data setup
Given the processed data described in Chapter 4, the relative velocities, ur, vr, at each sample, k, are
computed:
ur,k = uk −uc,k
vr,k = vk −vc,k
The problem considered is then estimating the dynamics of the ship given by the right hand side of
˙η = R(η) (νr + νc) ,

For simplicity the notation of time dependence is omitted from the variables in θu(t), θv(t), θr(t). The
problem is then identifying au, bv and cr.
To estimate the dynamics given by the coeﬀicients au, bv, cr, the accelerations ˙ur, ˙vr, ˙r are needed.
As these are not directly measured, one would need to estimate them by estimating the derivatives
of the velocities ur, vr, r. As the velocity measurements are subject to noise, the accelerations may be
estimated using for example a smoothed finite difference appproach with a Savitzky-Golay filter as done
in [Sil+20] or using total variation regularized derivate (TVRD) presented in [ROF92]. Alternatively, to
avoid the direct estimation of derivatives from noisy data, one may consider the discrete-time dynamical
system by applying a forward Euler-scheme to (5.15), such that
ur(tk + h) = ur(tk) + haT
u θu(tk),
(5.18a)
vr(tk + h) = vr(tk) + hbT
v θv(tk),
(5.18b)
r(tk + h) = r(tk) + hcT
r θr(tk),
(5.18c)
where tk = t0 + kh. As the data has a sampling time of 2 seconds, the lowest step size h available
from data, without further processing, is h = 2. One could potentially interpolate between samples
for all variables in the data to achieve a higher synthetic sampling rate, but as these would not be
true measurements it could result in dynamics being wrongly estimated and be contaminated with
noise. Note, this discretization is equivalent to estimating the derivatives using a simple forward finite
difference, but an advantage is that it also provides a simple integration scheme for use in simulation
and avoids the need for more complex ODE-solvers.
Other than avoiding the estimation of derivatives, a motivation for considering the discrete-time
system (5.18) instead of the continuous-time system (5.15) is that the ship is a relatively slow system.
This means forward Euler can be relatively accurate. This can be shown by examining the autocor-
relation of the velocity states, ur, vr and r. These can be seen in Figure 5.2. Here a boxplot off the
autocorrelation at each lag, 0 to 31, for the states ur, vr and r has been plotted to show the distribution
of the autocorrelation function across all transits. With a sample time of 2 seconds, lag 31 corresponds
to the autocorrelation with a sample from about 1 min before. Following traditional boxplot styles,
the blue box indicates the [25, 75]% percentiles, the horizontal dashed lines extend to the most extreme
data points not considered outliers, and the outliers are marked by the red ’+’ symbol. Figure 5.2(a)
shows this where the entire transit data, from Gedser to Rostock and vice versa, has been used. It can
be seen that the autocorrelation function decays very slowly for all three states for almost all transits,

thus indicating that the current sampled states are well described by the previous sampled states which
means the system is slow. Though it is still slowly decaying, the autocorrelation function for r is more
varying indicating that the yaw rate has the fastest time scale.
As the autocorrelation functions in Figure 5.2(a) are calculated based on the entire transits, they
are heavily influenced by the long open sea section of the transits where there is almost no change in ur,
vr and r. Hence the autocorrelation functions indicate slower behaviour than expected and does not
represent the system well. Thus, it would be more appropriate to examine the autocorrelation functions
for the transit data local to Gedser Harbour, where the ship is actually performing manoeuvres and
the data is more excited. This can be seen in Figure 5.2(b). Here, a faster decay for all states can be
observed. Most noticeable for vr and r, indicating that the ship operates at slightly faster timescales
than would be expected if considering the entire transit. Though, the decay is still relatively slow for all
states. Additionally, this indicates that the states operate at different time scales and a different step
size could potentially be used in the estimation of each state’s dynamics, but this will not be explored
further in this project.
To accommodate the discrete-time model (5.18), the data is for each transit, 1-54, split and arranged

where N is the number of samples in the transit. Note the difference between for example rk which
indicates the yaw rate measured at sample time tk in the data, as opposed to r(tk) which is r(t) evaluated
at time t = tk. As only the velocities are needed for estimation and not the position, all transits can be
used even the ones with mismatching position data as described in Section 4.2. Each matrix can then
be combined across all transits. By slightly abusing the notation, the estimation problem is then given
by finding au, bv, cr such that
U N

(5.19a)

It is important to mention that in the estimation of au, bv, cr, the coeﬀicients they consist of ai, cj, bk, i ∈
{1, . . . , 8}, j ∈{1, . . . , 15}, k ∈{1, . . . , 15} are assumed to be independent of each other, which in general
is not true. Some are indeed dependent through the physical constants of which they are constructed.
This assumption simplifies the estimation process at the cost of potentially violating some physical laws
and system constraints, and there not being an inverse transformation back to the original constants
used in the model derivation in Section 3.4.
As should have been made apparent by now, the different parts of a transit differ a lot in how well
the ship’s dynamics are described and excited in the data. For example, during open sea sailing there
is little to no manoeuvring and the motion consists mainly of straight, forward sailing. The control
inputs for the thrusters and propeller are mostly constant with small variations and it is mostly the
outside disturbances such as wind and current that vary here. As such, using the open sea data will
contribute very little in the estimation of a model capable of performing more advanced manoeuvres
than sailing straight. If the model were to be used for mostly straight, forward motion with close to
constant velocities, it would also be more appropriate to consider a more simple or linear model of the
states and inputs [Fos21]. Another key factor which differentiates open sea manoeuvring, manoeuvring
at Gedser and manoeuvring at Rostock is how confined the ship is, i.e. the depth below the ship and
the distance to banks. At open sea there is much larger depth beneath the ship, and banks can mostly
be assumed nonexistent. When close to harbour, the depth below the ship has a significant influence on
the manoeuvrability [Fos21] and both harbour and underwater banks are very present. From the data
and sea charts, it is also found that there is a large difference in water depth in Gedser Harbour (5-6
m) compared to Rostock Harbour (12-14 m). Additionally, as most of the transit is spent at open sea,
a model estimated using open sea data would be greatly biased towards performing well at open sea.
This was also confirmed during experiments, yielding higher manoeuvrability of the model than would
be expected in harbour. As one of the main objectives of this project is to design a controller capable of
performing the turning manoeuvre when leaving Gedser, it would be most suitable to train the model
==PAGE:46==
5.1 Support Vector Regression
39
on a more balanced data set where a larger fraction of the samples are local to Gedser. Thereby, for the
sake of estimating a model of the ship that is well suited for performing said manoeuvre, the training
data is truncated to only consist of the first 290 data points of transits leaving Gedser and the last 290
data points of transits arriving in Gedser. The position data for the truncated transits 34-53 can be
seen in Figure 5.3. Here it can be seen that this data only covers Gedser Harbour, the turning basin

500
Figure 5.3: Truncated position data for transit 34-53. Red is leaving Gedser. Yellow is arriving in
Gedser.
and a section of the fairway, varying from transit to transit.
A downside to only considering data local to Gedser is that modelling precision in the estimation of
the centre propeller and main rudder dynamics is lost, as they are mostly used in the fairway and at
open sea. However, since they are mainly used there, the consequence of them being poorly estimated
should not be of importance for future results. Another downside to only considering data local to
Gedser is that some generalisability of the ship dynamics outside of Gedser is lost; for example at
Rostock. As such, an update of the model parameters could be necessary if the model were to be used
to design a controller for the harbour in Rostock. This would compensate for the discrepancies between
Gedser and Rostock.
5.1.3
Applying SVR
It is desired to estimate the coeﬀicients au, bv, cr in the discrete-time system (5.18). For this purpose
SVR will be used. In this project, a linear kernel (5.12) is used. A linear kernel is used instead of a
polynomial or Gaussian kernel because the system structure is known beforehand and the parameters to
be estimated appear linearly in the system model. Polynomial and Gaussian kernels work well for iden-
tifying unknown nonlinear systems [Che+22] as they estimate the parameters in a higher-dimensional
feature space given by the kernel and the accompanying mapping, φ. However, a linear kernel should
suﬀice by the preceding arguments. In addition, using a linear kernel makes the problem computation-
ally simpler to solve. Since φ easy to compute for the linear kernel, the weights can be found directly.
This can be done whether the optimisation problem is solved in the primal or the dual formulation.
As the mapping, φ, may be diﬀicult to compute for nonlinear kernels, it is most common to evaluate
the estimated function using the kernel. This means that an explicit expression for the weights is not
commonly used. As such, if an explicit expression for the weight is required, it can be undesirable to use
nonlinear kernels as the analysis and evaluation of φ may be cumbersome. Therefore, a linear kernel
becomes the suitable choice as an explicit expression for the weights is required for the control design
in later chapters. Lastly, it is best to solve the optimisation problem in its primal formulation, as more
observations than variables are available (N ≫m).
==PAGE:47==
5.1 Support Vector Regression
40
The estimation of au, bv, cr is done independently and is therefore split into three optimisation problems

Note, that the estimation of the bias term is omitted, as a non-zero bias would result in an unstable
model at zero velocity and zero input. Including it would almost surely produce better predictions, but
would not coincide with how a ship should behave, hence it is omitted. Additionally, λ is used instead
of C, e.g. λu instead of Cu, which is equivalent to using Cu = 1/λu.
In each optimisation problem only one hyperparameter is tuned, respectively the parameters λu, λv
and λr. As described earlier, these control the trade-off between the penalty for observations outside
the ϵ-margin and the regularisation of au, bv and cr. To tune these a 5-fold cross-validation scheme is
used [HTF08]. Other k-folds were tried as well, but a 5-fold yielded the best results in general. One may
also choose to tune ϵ controlling the ϵ-margin, and it has been shown that there is a linear dependency
between the noise level in the data and the optimal ϵ-parameter for SVR [SSS04]. This does however
require knowledge of the noise model, which is not available. Furthermore, there exist methods to
automatically adjust and tune ϵ by adding it as a variable of the optimisation problem [SSS04]. This
will not be considered, as suﬀiciently good results were achieved by choosing ϵ as an approximation of
the standard deviation of the observations based on the interquartile range [MAT23].
For solving the linear SVR optimisation problems (5.20), MATLABs function fitrlinear, which
fits linear regression models, is used. Following best practices of model estimation, the training data is
normalised using max-abs scaling, x′ = x/ max(|x|), to preserve sign-sensitive data which most likely
hold important information for the estimation. After a model has been fitted the estimated parameters
are transformed back to the unnormalised space by using the inverse of the normalisation scale.
To assess the performance of the SVR model estimate, it is compared with a model estimated
using the closely related and well-established least-squares (LS) regression method. The least-squares
regression method is equivalent to using ϵ = 0 and no regularisation in the linear SVR (5.2). Additionally,
it is compared with the Identity model. The Identity model is defined by the prediction of the next state
being the current state, e.g. rk+1 = rk. This corresponds to all model coeﬀicients being 0. The Identity
model will function as a baseline to conclude whether the data is even excited enough to determine a
model that can properly describe the dynamics of the ship.
Transit 1 through 52 is used as training/validation data for training the model and finding optimal
hyperparameters in 5-fold cross-validation. Transit 53 is a transit leaving Gedser and transit 54 is a
transit arriving in Gedser, and will be used as test transits to evaluate the performance of the models
on unseen data. As such, they will be put aside and only be considered and used after the model has
been trained on transit 1-52.

for the training data are of order 10−4 as indicated in the top left of Table 5.1(a). Compared to the
