Identity model, both LS and SVR improve prediction accuracy for all three states, especially in ur.
Slightly lower training error in all states for the least-squares model is obtained compared to the SVR.
This is expected as least squares is known to produce the lowest variance of all unbiased linear models
[HTF08]. This means that LS may also overfit to training data and have reduced prediction ability on
unseen data. As such it is better to compare the models on the test data.
Figure 5.4 shows the Identity, LS and SVR models’ one-step prediction ability in the three states
on the outgoing transit 53 and ingoing transit 54. As mentioned earlier, the two transits have not been
trained on. The two left columns show the prediction and prediction error for the three states for the
outgoing transit 53. The two right columns show the prediction and prediction error for the three states
for the ingoing transit 54. For both transits, no immediate difference in the models can be seen in the
predictions, and they all seem to predict well. This can be explained by the slowness of the system and
how well the next state, k+1, is described by the current state, k. The difference in the models’ ability to
predict is much more apparent when examining the prediction error. In general, the LS model and SVR
model predict very similarly, producing almost the same prediction error. Only small deviations occur
throughout both transits. It can be seen that in ur a large portion of the dynamics are not captured by
the Identity model. These are however estimated well by the LS and SVR models, producing a lower
prediction error, though some small trends can still be observed. In vr for the outgoing transit, the LS
and SVR also produce better results than the Identity model, but there are still some dynamics that
have not been captured. This is seen as trends in the prediction error. For the ingoing transit, there
is very little variation in vr. Thus all models predict equally well and produce similar prediction errors.
In r the prediction errors are also hard to differentiate between models, but the LS and SVR can be
observed to generally produce lower prediction errors. For the ingoing transit, this can be explained
by not much happening in the turn rate. For the outgoing transit, it may be caused by the dynamics
not being described accurately enough by the variables in θr, though they do correctly identify some
dynamics. In general, it is seen the most significant prediction errors for the LS and SVR occur during
the turning manoeuvre (k = 90 . . . 210) for the outgoing transit.
The performance of the models’ prediction ability can be more compactly summarised by the result-
ing mean square errors. Table 5.2 shows the thereto MSE for the 1-step prediction error for the three
models for the outgoing and ingoing transit, and the MSE of combining the two transits. Here, lower
MSEs are produced by the LS and SVR models compared to the Identity model. The exception being
vr for the ingoing transit, which was not very excited. Again, the largest increase in accuracy is for the
surge velocity ur. Comparing the LS and SVR models, the SVR model performs slightly better in ur
and r for both transits, while the LS model only performs slightly better in vr for the outgoing transit.
All in all, the LS model and the SVR model perform very similarly with only minor differences.
This may be explained by the ϵ-parameters in the SVR model being relatively small due to the large

5.2
Sparse Identification of Nonlinear Dynamics
This section presents a general description of the theory and ideas behind a discrete formulation of
the Sparse Identification of Nonlinear Dynamics (SINDy) [BPK16]. Thereafter a presentation of how
SINDy is applied in this project and the accompanying results are given.
5.2.1
Theory
Consider a continuous nonlinear dynamical system of the form

x(0) = x0,

associated discrete-time formulation of (5.22) be given by
xk+1 = f(xk, uk),
x(0) = x0.
(5.22)

As the assumption of SINDy states that the coeﬀicients Ξ are mostly sparse, sparse regression can
be employed to identify Ξ giving the fewest nonlinear functions in the library Θ used to describe the
governing equations. Note, each column of Ξ requires a separate optimisation problem to find the sparse
vector of coeﬀicients ξi, i = 1, . . . , n. A well established sparse regression method which works well for
this problem is LASSO. LASSO is a shrinkage method [HTF08] and utilises the L1-norm for penalising
nonzero entries in ξi, thus yielding sparse solutions. The sparse regression problem is thereby given by

coeﬀicients.
5.2.2
Applying SINDy
In this project, SINDy will be used for capturing potentially unmodelled dynamics, that may not
have been captured in the estimated SVR model Section 5.1.2. To apply SINDy, the data needs to
be appropriately organised and an appropriate library of candidate functions needs to be defined for
capturing potentially unmodelled dynamics.

functions Θ needs to be determined.
5.2.2.2
Candidate Functions Exploration
SINDy is heavily dependent on the nonlinear functions used in the library, Θ, therefore some exploration
of relevant candidate functions is needed. Section 3.4.1 gives a general and superficial description of
additional hydrodynamic forces not taken into consideration in the model (3.5). These include squat
effects, bank effects and shallow and restricted water effects which are present in and close to Gedser
harbour. As the SVR model is based of (3.5), these hydrodynamic forces are not directly modelled.
Thus, considering candidate functions related to squat effects, bank effects and shallow and restricted
water effects could provide additional important unmodelled terms for explaining the dynamics of the
ship. However, it should be noted that some of these effects may be captured indirectly by the SVR
model given that it is trained on data primarily from Gedser Harbour and the turning basin, thus
leading to a decrease in effectiveness of the thrusters and in the manoeuvrability than if trained on
open sea data.
As mentioned in Section 3.4.1, [Gou11] and [LVD18] shows that squat-, bank- and similar harbour
effects are inversely proportional to the depth and distances to banks and proportional to the velocities
squared. Similarly, [Tan+20] shows that the effectiveness of thrusters can be modelled as being inversely
proportional to the depth and distances to banks. Hence, important variables in data for estimating the
unmodelled hydrodynamic effects are the distances from ship to banks, the depth under keel and the
velocities of the ship. The depth below the transducer is given in data and the distances from ship to
bank are computed using the positional data and an outline map of Gedser Harbour with the turning
basin, as described in Section 4.2. The depth and distances readily available are thereby:

where
θ(k) = [ur, vr, r, |ur|, |vr|, |r|, u2
r, v2
r, r2, urvr, urr, ur|ur|, ur|vr|,
ur|r|, vrr, vr|ur|, vr|vr|, vr|r|, r|ur|, r|vr|, r|r|]T .
(5.33)
This library consists of a total of 147 candidate functions and is a function of only the states and
distances and not the thruster inputs such as RPM and angles. An extension of this library could then
be to also include the thruster inputs. These can be included by multiplying the thruster inputs in the
SVR variables (5.17) with the inverse of the depth and distances. As such, the generated candidate
functions would estimate adjustments to the coeﬀicients of the SVR model terms based on the depth
and distances. This also means that the library differs for each state as the thruster inputs does not
affect each state the same. Each state having different libraries will not affect the regression problem in
any meaningful way. The resulting libraries, denoted Θu, Θv, Θr for ur, vr, r, of this extension consists
of 168, 196 and 196 nonlinear candidate functions, respectively. Further extensions of the libraries could
potentially be explored, but the size of the libraries would also increase drastically with more terms
included. Thus, only the two libraries mentioned will be considered.
5.2.3
Results
The same data local to Gedser that was used to train the SVR model is used to estimate ξu, ξv and
ξr and optimise hyperparameters in the SINDy regression problem (5.31). Now, only transit 34-54 can
be used due to transit 1-33 having mismatching positional data and the positional data is needed to
compute the distances to the banks. This means that the SINDy regression have approximately half the
data available compared to the SVR. Again, transit 53 and 54 are used as test transits to evaluate the
performance. This means that both the SVR model and the SINDy extension have not been trained
on transit 53 and 54. Hence the two transits can be used to asses the combined performance. To
evaluate the performance of the SVR with the SINDy extension, it is compared to only using the SVR
model. This is done in order to see if an increase in the accuracy of the ship dynamics is achieved with
the SINDy extension. Both the candidate library that only contains functions of the states and the
candidate library that also contains functions of the inputs are tested to see if a substantial increase
in accuracy can be achieved by including the input terms. These two extensions will respectively be
referred to as ”SINDy” and ”SINDy+” in the following text, tables and figures. As per best practices,
the data is normalised before solving the SINDy regression problem, the same way it was for the SVR
regression problem.
The training error, given by the mean square one-step prediction error, for the SVR model without
and with the SINDy extensions can be seen in Table 5.4(a). The optimised hyperparameters for SINDy
and SINDy+ are shown in Table 5.4(b) and the fraction of non-zero coeﬀicients for each model is shown
in Table 5.4(c). It will not be stated exactly which terms are the non-zero ones. As indicated in the top

left of Table 5.4(a), the MSEs for the training data is of order 10−3. Compared to the SVR model both
SINDy and SINDy+ improve the training prediction accuracy slightly. It can be seen from Table 5.4(c)
that the SINDy model does not include any terms in ur, hence why there is no improvement in the
training error compared to the SVR model. For the SINDy+ model 9 extra terms are added, which is
acceptable. For vr only a small improvement in the training MSE is achieved even though 17/19 extra
terms are added. This is a somewhat acceptable number of extra terms, but it could prove challenging
to use in a control environment. For r the improvement is slightly more significant improvement in the
training MSE is achieved, but also 77/108 extra terms are added. This is an unacceptable amount of
extra terms and it would be almost infeasible to consider them all in a control design. The high number
of terms are also indicated by the values of ˆλu, ˆλv and ˆλr in Table 5.4(b); Meaning a less and less sparse
vector of coeﬀicients is needed in order to properly explain the unmodelled dynamics in r compared to
ur and vr.
Figure 5.5 shows the SVR, SINDy and SINDy+ models’ one-step prediction ability in the three
states on the outgoing transit 53 and ingoing transit 54. As mentioned earlier, the two transits have
not been trained on. The two left columns show the prediction and prediction error respectively for the
three states for the outgoing transit 53. The two right columns show the prediction and prediction error
respectively for the three states for the ingoing transit 54. Again, no immediate difference in the models
can be seen in the predictions for both transits and they all seem to predict well. The difference in the
models ability to predict is again much more apparent when examining the prediction error. Generally,
the SINDy and SINDy+ models perform very similar to the SVR model, providing little improvement
in the predictions. Again, for the ingoing transit there is very little variation in ur, vr and r, thus all
models predict almost equally well and produce similar prediction error. For the outgoing transit, in ur
there is still a portion of the dynamics that are not captured by the SINDy extensions, though SINDy+
seems to slightly improve the predictions in some areas. The same can be seen in vr, but SINDy now
also improve the predictions in the same areas as SINDy+. In r, from the prediction errors it is also
hard to make any meaningful conclusion about the differences between models.
The resulting mean square one-step prediction error in Table 5.2 can then be examined to see if the
above observation are correct. From these, the same observations can be made; SINDy+ provides a
slight improvement in MSE in ur compared to the SVR and SINDy models. In vr, both SINDy and
SINDy+ provide a minor improvement in MSE but both are very close. In r, the improvement is also
minor, but a slightly better performance can be seen for the SINDy+ model than the SINDy model.

5.2.4
SINDy Conclusion
From these results it seems that the two SINDy extensions provide very little improvement in prediction
accuracy compared to what the SVR model already provides. The improvement is not significant in the
places where it is most needed, i.e., in the turning where the SVR model is weakest at predictions. The
minor improvements also come at a large cost in model complexity due to the many additional terms
needed to describe the dynamics.
Multiple strategies were tried to increase the accuracy of the SINDy models. These include imple-
menting SISC as in [AWC22] using the FORCE simulator as a co-teacher, implementing MSINDY as
in [KBN22] to estimate noise, implementing Robust SINDy with rapid model recovery as in [Qua+18]
to adaptively update which terms are active in SINDy. Additionally, it was tried to set up other can-
didate function libraries in the hopes that a different set of candidate functions could provide better
representation of the dynamics. However, none of these strategies yielded better results than the ones
presented.
One of the reasons for this is probably due to the SVR model already capturing a lot of the dynamics
which are local to Gedser since the data trained on has been limited to Gedser.
As such, trying
to estimate them using SINDy does not yield any significantly better accuracy. It was also tried to
model the SVR using data outside Gedser and then use SINDy on top of that model to capture the local
dynamics, but this did not yield any better results. Also, as the bank and squat effects are mostly active
and affect the ship at higher speeds, the captain has most likely already taken this into consideration
when manoeuvring the ship through restricted waters and thereby slowed down appropriately. Thus,
the effects may not be as present as hoped for in the data for a modelling purpose. That the SINDy
model yields a very large number of nonzero terms in yaw also means that it greatly increases model
==PAGE:56==

complexity which is not desired. Especially when designing controllers. Hence, a SINDy model of the
unmodelled dynamics was not achieved. The theory was that only a few terms would be active, but
the results show that this is not possible with the current model if the accuracy also needs to increase
suﬀiciently with the additional terms. As such, an SVR model without SINDy will considered in future
sections and chapters.
As will be explained in the Section 5.3.1, it was late in the project found that a large portion of
the model inaccuracies could perhaps be explained by an inaccurate representation and modelling of
the bow thrusters. Fixing this could potentially help in providing better results of using SINDy for
estimating the unmodelled dynamics.
5.3
Final Model Validation & Discussion
During the initial control tests, it was found that the SVR model estimated from the ship data given
by the model coeﬀicients in Table 5.3 did not adequately describe the ship simulated in the FORCE
Technology’s simulator. This was discovered through a large mismatch between expected behaviour
given by the estimated SVR model and the actual behaviour of the ship in the simulator. This problem
was especially noticeable during turning of the ship. The mismatch was further confirmed through
testing of manoeuvres such as comparison of turning circles, comparison of acceleration and speed
when using azipods and comparison of turning capabilities using only bow thrusters.
It was tried to tune the controllers to better regulate in the FORCE simulator in the presence of the
model mismatch, but it had a too significant negative impact on the quality of the designed controllers.
Additionally, the mismatch was deemed too great for an adaptive control design to work.
The mismatch was believed to be caused by insuﬀicient excitation of the actuators in the data
(Though it was late in the project discovered to be more likely caused by inaccurate modelling of the
bow thrusters; see Section 5.3.1.1 and the disclaimers below.). As such, additional data with more
excitation was deemed necessary to obtain an adequate model. However, such data was not readily
available from the actual ship.
Fortunately, a new SVR model could be estimated using data from the FORCE simulator with additional
data from manoeuvrability tests to add excitation. Specifically, three tests are added: one isolating
the effects of the individual azipods, one isolating the effects of the bow thrusters and one isolating the
effects of wind.
The input sequences for the first two tests are plotted in Figure 5.6. The last test simulates a wind

additional transits.
One can consider the pros and cons of estimating a model based on data from simulating the ship
in the FORCE simulator versus data from actual transits of the ship:
==PAGE:58==
5.3 Final Model Validation & Discussion
51
Inherent to real data is the presence of noise and for system identification having noisy data can greatly
impact the model estimation. If the data is too dominated by noise, a reasonable model can be very
diﬀicult to obtain. Though if a noise estimate or a model of the noise is available, very precise models
can still be estimated. It is also possible for an estimated model to be over fit when trained on data with
noise. Hence, the estimated model tries to capture and predict the noise behaviour, which in general is
not predictable. This can of course be taken care of by different methods, such as regularisation.
This is not the case for data from the simulator.
Here, noise can be artificially added but at the
core everything is exactly known and the ”measured” values are true. Having measurements with no
noise can significantly impact model estimation. As mentioned, without noise the observations are true
observations, meaning the model can be very well estimated since the only real inaccuracy is inaccurate
modelling. Also, an advantage of using the simulator is that data can be readily generated as done
here with the actuators. Getting data for such tests from the real ship would be time consuming and
expensive. This means that if the data is deemed insuﬀicient, new data can be provided in a very fast
and inexpensive manner.
All this is of course under the assumption that the data from the simulator is equivalent to data from
the real ship. It is true that the FORCE simulator is very well representative of the actual ship, but any
dynamics and phenomena not modelled, not included or only estimated in the simulator will contribute
to a mismatch from the actual ship and hence also a mismatch in the simulator data. As such, the SVR
model estimated from simulator data will be a model of a model of the ship. Thus, any inaccuracies in
the simulator model will also be present in the SVR model in addition to the model inaccuracies between
the SVR model and the simulator. Since it is not possible to test on the actual ship, a quantification
and/or modelling of these mismatches could be hard to achieve. Therefore, it must be assumed that
the FORCE simulator can be seen as the true ship in order for the SVR model trained on simulator
data to be valid. This also goes for the control designs using the SVR model.
• Disclaimer 1: The results presented in this and all subsequent sections assume that the input
to the bow thrusters in the FORCE simulator are given as a fraction of RPM. Here 1 corresponds
to maximum RPM in the starboard direction and −1 corresponds to maximum RPM in the port
direction. The values between −1 and 1 are then converted back to RPM values between −100 and
100 by a simple scaling. These erroneous results are presented in various sections and figures. The
reality is that the value between −1 and 1 indicates a lever command that is converted through
a combinator to an RPM value and a pitch value. Hence the bow thruster RPM values should be
converted to a lever command by the reader using a simple scaling. The real relationship between
lever command and RPM is unclear but could potentially be nonlinear. The error will not be
corrected as it was found late in the writing of this thesis.
• Disclaimer 2: The model estimated using the data generated by the FORCE simulator assumed
that the output values for the bow thrusters’ actuation level were between −100 and 100 RPM.
The reality is that they were values between −1 and 1, corresponding to lever commands. This
means that all the added data from the simulator related to the bow thrusters contained erroneous
values, potentially biasing towards worse results. This error was found late in the writing of the
thesis, which is why it will not be corrected.
5.3.1
Assessing Model Accuracy
To get a better estimate of the inaccuracies of the final model, it is desired to quantify the unmodelled
dynamics when compared to the FORCE simulator. Doing this could potentially lead to discovering
the most significant unmodelled dynamics and a more robust controller design. Here, a sliding mode
approach is applied to estimate the unmodelled dynamics directly. Such an estimate could, in theory,
be feed-forwarded to achieve disturbance rejection. This has been deemed out of scope in this thesis;
the estimate is solely used for discussion purposes.
Moreover, the technique applied exploits that
the velocities from FORCE Technology’s simulator are exactly known, thus not applicable in practice
without modifications.
==PAGE:59==
5.3 Final Model Validation & Discussion
52
To this end, collect the right hand side of (5.15) in
F (η, νr, u) =


aT
u θu(t)
bT
v θv(t)
cT
r θr(t)

,
where u contains thruster RPM values and azipod angles. Then, the velocity dynamics may be written
as
˙νr = F (η, νr, u) + d(t),
where d(t) contain any unmodelled dynamics. Let
˙ˆνr,i = Fi(η, νr, u) + ki sgn(˜νr,i) for i = 1, 2, 3,
be estimates of the relative velocities, and ˜νr,i = νr,i −ˆνr,i be the corresponding estimation errors with
time derivatives
˙˜νr,i = di(t) −ki sgn(˜νr,i) for i = 1, 2, 3.
(5.34)
It may seem counter-intuitive to estimate the relative velocities since their exact values are assumed to
be available. However, if all ki are chosen such that (5.34) satisfies Theorem 4, then
ki sgn(˜νr,i) = di(t),
after some finite time Ti. This result relies upon the infinitely high-frequency switching of the sign
function, thus not useful in practice. Nevertheless, an approximation can be obtained by substituting
the sign function with the saturation function; see Corollary 2. That is, inside the boundary layer
ˆνr,i ≤ϕi,
ˆdi(t) = ki sat(˜νr,i) ≈di(t),
constitutes estimates of the unmodelled dynamics in each dimension.
The relative velocities, νr, are extracted from FORCE Technology’s simulator performing the turning
manoeuvring. These are then estimated using the above, and the estimation error is used to compute
ˆd(t); see Figure 5.7. From this, it can be seen that the estimated relative velocities track their real
counterparts almost immediately. Hence, ˆd(t) constitutes a reliable estimate from the first time instance.
The turning manoeuvre is performed in the time interval between 200 and 400 seconds. Within this
interval, the unmodelled dynamics reach a significant magnitude, particularly in surge and sway. Notice
that the model inaccuracies are low during the initial acceleration, where the azipods are angled parallel
to the heading to produce backwards surge velocity. In contrast, the azipods are not close to parallel
to the heading during the turning manoeuvre. Moreover, the bow thrusters are only active during the
turning manoeuvre. These observations suggest that, even with extra tests performed in Section 5.3, the
identified model does not capture the complex dynamics during the turning manoeuvre. In particular,
the model may not accurately describe the effect of the bow thrusters or the azipods when the they are
not close to parallel to the heading.
5.3.1.1
Assessing Accuracy of Bow Thrusters
The analysis in Section 5.3.1 suggested that the bow thrusters are not suﬀiciently modelled. This is
further explored here, where the identified model is compared to FORCE Technology’s simulator when
applying the bow thrusters in different scenarios. Specifically, two levels of actuation, 50 RPM and 75
RPM, at two different surge velocities, 0 m/s and 2 m/s, will be considered. Of interest is the short-term
velocity effect of applying the bow thruster in these scenarios; see Figure 5.8. When the ship is initially
stationary, the identified model underestimates the bow thrusters’ effect when they run at 50 RPM in
both sway and yaw. Interestingly, the underestimation is less severe when the bow thrusters run at 75
RPM, but at this actuation level, the identified model significantly overestimates the resulting surge

velocity. When in forward motion, the bow thrusters’ effect in both sway and yaw diminishes, which
is correctly modelled in FORCE Technology’s simulator. However, the model does not capture this
behaviour, leading to significant overestimates of the resulting sway and yaw at both actuation levels.
Overall, the identified model generally underestimates the bow thruster effect at lower velocities
and overestimates their effect at higher velocities. This inaccuracy affects nearly all other aspects of
the thesis, but its significance was discovered too late in the project for a solution to be implemented.
Nonetheless, some solution suggestions will be presented.
Recall that Section 5.3 added additional
transits to increase excitation, particularly a transit isolating the bow thrusters at different velocities
between 0 and 2 m/s. Modifying this transit, such that only lower velocities are considered, could
potentially reduce the underestimation effect at lower velocities. Furthermore, if the bow thrusters are
restricted to operate under these velocities, then the model may adequately capture their performance.
One could also make the bow thruster terms dependent on the velocity, such that their effect diminishes
at higher velocities. This could capture the thrusters’ performance at all velocities, assuming a suitable
relationship between the bow thrusters and the velocity can be identified. This thesis applies a partial
solution to the problem: the thrusters’ actuation at higher velocities is limited without modifying the
transits as above. The solution is only partial; it reduces unnecessary actuation at higher velocities,
but it does not address the issues caused by underestimating their effect at lower velocities.

CHAPTER 6
Guidance System
The concept of the guidance system used in this thesis is very much inspired by Fossen [Fos21]. The
idea is to create a route for the ship to follow that is optimal in some sense. The focus will primarily
be on time minimisation, control input (and hence energy consumption) and the ease for which the
controller can follow the route, with emphasis on the last two.
Guidance System
RRT*
Model Based
Optimal Routing
Weather Data
Model Estimate
Reference
Signal
Figure 6.1: An overview of the guidance system.
To create a guidance system, one must first understand what route planning is about.
Route
planning is often understood as a search in state space, N , for a route from an initial state, ηinit, to
a goal state, ηgoal [LaV98]. There are several ways of obtaining this. Most of the read literature does
not consider the task of manoeuvring in restricted waters. It assumes that the ship is manoeuvring
in open waters. This is not readily applicable for the purpose of this project. Hence with inspiration
from robotics [Buc+19; Buc+21], this chapter will consider whole-body route planning. As such it is
natural to define an obstacle region, N obs ⊂N , that must be avoided. Unlike other route planning
strategies, whole-body route planning considers the entire body of the controlled object by iteratively
performing collision detection between the control object and its surroundings. This naturally leads
to the definition of the obstacle-free region, N free = N ∁
obs, i.e. the complement of the obstacle region.
A route is then understood as a series of poses ηi ∈N free, i = 0, 1, . . . , N such that η0 = ηinit and
ηN = ηgoal. Note that the line segment between two subsequent poses in the route must also lie in
N free [LaV98].
A simple approach would be using the routes supplied from data. However, these are not readily
applicable as the poses along with the linear and angular velocities are very noisy. Hence the second
derivatives are diﬀicult to estimate accurately.
As is also seen later, the approach outlined in this
chapter is adaptable in the sense that it can handle weather changes and changes in obstacle locations.
The latter being very useful for use in other harbours or other restricted waters.
The guidance system presented here creates a route that satisfies some predefined constraints, partic-
ularly constraints enforcing the identified dynamics and allowing higher order derivatives to be computed
explicitly. The guidance system is a cascaded system; it consists of a sampling based method presented
in Section 6.1 that creates an initial route, which is then cascaded into an optimisation based method
presented in Section 6.2 that creates the final route. An overview of this can be seen in Figure 6.1. The
two subsystems will be presented as individual guidance systems in their respective sections. Afterwards,
they are combined when routes are generated in Section 6.3.
==PAGE:63==
6.1 Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree
56
6.1
Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree
Rapidly-Exploring Random Trees (RRTs) is a sampling based approach to route planning. An RRT is
a random tree, T , that is constructed in such a manner that it will rapidly explore the obstacle-free
region, N free. Hence, it can be used for route planning as you can start it at ηinit and let it run until
a suitable point near ηgoal is found or until a certain amount of iterations have been performed. Often
the latter is chosen. Then it is simply a matter of connecting ηinit with ηgoal through points in the tree.
The algorithm (adopted from [LaV98]) can be seen in Algorithm 1. The way it works is by randomly
sampling a point, ηrand ∈N free. Then it finds the nearest neighbour to the random point already in
the tree, ηnear. The algorithm then tries to move the system from ηnear towards ηrand. The result is
saved in ηnew, which is added to the tree if the movement between the tree and the new point is valid.
Valid is understood as not in collision with N obs and adhering to system dynamics. An illustration of
the algorithm can be seen in Figure 6.2(a)-6.2(d). It is possible for system dynamics to be incorporated
into the function NEW_STATE using some discretisation scheme. Say the system dynamics follows,
˙η = f(η, u),
then system dynamics could be included by letting NEW_STATE compute,
η(t + ∆t) ≈F(η(t), u(t), ∆t),
for some discretisation scheme given by F and ∆t [LaV98; LK01]. Examples of discretisation schemes
are Euler integration or Runge-Kutta methods. [LK01] used RRT to generate seemingly good paths for
many different dynamical models with up to 12 degrees of freedom. Note that the generated paths were
created with no guarantee of being optimal or near-optimal. Optimal meaning a feasible path with the
smallest possible cost. this will be discussed later.
The simplest method of growing the tree towards the randomly sampled point is using Euler inte-
gration [LaV98]
F(η(t), u(t), ∆t) = η(t) + u(t)
where u must be chosen such that it moves the system towards the randomly sampled point and
∥u∥Q =
Q
1
2 u

2 ≤d. Here d > 0 is the connection-distance parameter that will be explained later
and Q is some positive-definite weight matrix. This would be applicable for holonomic systems, i.e.
systems where states do not depend on the movement there. A choice like this would mean that the
system would be able to obtain any bounded velocity. One could also consider a model, which captures
the essentials of ship dynamics: forward and backwards movement with rotation of the ship itself. This
simple model is given by,
ηk+1 = ηk +

Constraints on u1 and u2 would need to reflect the dynamics of the true system. As the methods are
random, a lot of computational effort is used. The choice of this model reduces this by being simpler
to evaluate and control.

6.1.1
Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree*
The RRT algorithm presented in the previous section has no guarantee of optimal or optimal-like
solutions. [KF11] shows that the RRT algorithm returns sub-optimal solutions with probability one
in the limit, K →∞. This means that RRT algorithms will almost surely be sub-optimal. [KF11]
then introduces several optimal algorithms including Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree* (RRT*), which
is shown to be asymptotically optimal, i.e. almost surely returns an optimal path as K →∞. The
way RRT* overcomes the problems of RRT is by incremental rewiring of the tree [GSB14]. When a
new node is added, nearby nodes are considered as candidate parent nodes, which are chosen based on
minimum cost. Candidate children nodes are also considered and rewired based on the same principle.
The connecting of nodes along a minimum-cost path and the tree rewiring will be denoted as tree
refinement.
The algorithm (adopted from [KF10; KF11]) can be seen in Algorithm 2. It adds nodes like the
RRT algorithm. The difference here is that it also searches an area with radius r around the new node,
ηnew, for other nodes in the tree. Then by computing the cost to each node around ηnew it chooses
the optimal parent node. The cost is assumed additive and is computed as a line cost, i.e. a weighted
2-norm of the vector between the nodes, v,
∥v∥Q =
Q
1
2 v

2 .
(6.2)
Afterwards it considers candidate children nodes. The cost is computed similarly to before and the
children node with the smallest cost is added as a child to the new node, ηnew. The distance r is based
on the assumption of the theorem in [KF11] for RRT* to be asymptotically optimal,
r = min

γRRT ∗·
log(card(τ))
card(τ)
 1



Here d is a connection-distance parameter, which is the maximum distance between nodes in the tree.
Furthermore, n = dim(N ) is the dimension of the state space and card(τ) is the cardinality (i.e. number
of nodes) of the tree, τ. The intuitive idea behind this choice of r is that the connection distance should
decrease as the amount of nodes in the tree increase. γRRT ∗is a constant chosen as,

6.2 Model Based Optimal Routing
60
6.1.2
Collision Detection
Both the RRT and the RRT* algorithm depend on the function IS_VALID, which also performs collision
detection. It takes as input two states and determines whether the linear path between the states is
valid.
The collision detection algorithm for the RRT and RRT* algorithm is very simple and is based
on the polygons of the harbour and ship described in Section 3.5. The detection algorithm works by
computing the area of the intersection between the harbour polygon and the ship polygon. For nonzero
area, the ship and harbour are defined to be in collision. The collision detection algorithm starts out
by linearly interpolating k = 10 states between the two given states, i.e.
x1→2,j = x1 +
j
k −1(x2 −x1),
j = 0, 1, . . . , (k −1)
(6.4)
for interpolation between the states, x1 and x2.
Here x1→2,j denotes the j’th interpolation point
between x1 and x2. Afterwards it checks if all the interpolated states are in collision. If they are not
in collision, the algorithm returns true. If some of the states are in collision, the algorithm returns
false. An illustration of the collision detection can be seen in Figure 6.3. Note that the difference in
the two states has been greatly exaggerated. The actual distance is less than the connection-distance
parameter, d.
(a) The collision detection algorithm returns false as
some interpolated states are in collision.
(b) The collision detection algorithm returns true as
all interpolated states are not in collision.
Figure 6.3: An illustration of the collision detection algorithm. Green is a state not in collision and
red is a state in collision. The collision detection algorithm returns true if all the k = 10 interpolated
states between two nodes are not in collision, i.e. green.
6.2
Model Based Optimal Routing
RRT based methods can find a feasible route in a nonconvex setting, however, the routes generated
showed erratic behaviour, such as unnecessary and undesirable oscillations in heading1. The undesirable
1These oscillations may be caused by the wrong implementation of the IS_VALID function. This is discussed in
Section 6.3.1.3.
==PAGE:68==
6.2 Model Based Optimal Routing
61
behaviour can be alleviated by incorporating the identified dynamics within a constrained optimisation
problem. By doing this, the ship’s capabilities can be accounted for, and any undesirable behaviour
can be penalised. Techniques within dynamic optimisation will here be applied to develop Model Based
Optimal Routing (MBOR), an approach for optimal reference creation.
Eﬀicient optimisation solvers are often gradient-based and rely on the differentiability of the objective
and all constraints to find local optimal solutions. Consequently, a more advanced collision detection
method is required, as the area based method is not differentiable everywhere.
Such a method is
developed in Section 6.2.1 before the MBOR approach is presented in Section 6.2.2.
6.2.1
Differentiable Collision Detection
[THM23] presents DCOL - a differentiable conic optimisation approach for pairwise collision detection
between six types of convex primitives in R3. When restricted to two convex polygons in the plane,
DCOL simplifies to solving a linear program on the form:

inequality

minimum scaling coeﬀicient, α, such that the two polygons have at least a single point in common,
or equivalently, such that they are colliding. In other words, the polygons can be scaled down while
maintaining collision if α ≤1, and conversely, must be scaled up to introduce a collision if α > 1. Thus,
the polygons collide if and only if α ≤1. The Lagrangian of (6.5) is
L(α, x, z, θ) = α + zT

allows fast gradient evaluation, which can improve the eﬀiciency of many optimisation solvers.
The DCOL method is limited to collisions between convex polygons and cannot be directly applied
to detect collisions between the ship and the nonconvex outline. To circumvent this problem, the outline
is triangulated by partitioning it into N triangles, as seen in Figure 6.4(a). Each triangle in the partition
is a convex polygon and can be parameterised on the form

Solving (6.7) for each triangle is ineﬀicient when using off-the-shelf linear programming solvers, which
are often designed to solve larger programs. [THM23] develops a custom interior point solver specifically
optimised for the small programs involved in DCOL. However, their implementation is written in Julia
and would have to be rewritten in MATLAB to be applied in this thesis. One could also minimise
the number of programs on the form (6.7), by finding the smallest set of convex polygons covering
the outline. This problem is NP-hard, but an approximate solution is presented in [EW01]. Again,
a MATLAB implementation is not readily available, and the algorithm was deemed too complex to

(6.8)
The pairs (xi, αi) in (6.8) are independent in the sense that adjustments to one pair does not affect the
feasibility of the others. Hence, minimising the sum of scaling coeﬀicients is equivalent to minimising
each individually. Solving (6.8) using MATLAB’s linprog solver takes approximately the same time as
solving one of the N programs on the form (6.7).
6.2.2
MBOR Approach
MBOR generates a continuous reference in two steps. First, an optimal route satisfying the identified
dynamics (5.15) is found by solving an optimisation problem. The optimal route is then interpolated
using a cubic spline, resulting in a continuous reference signal. This approach has several benefits:
• The chance of saturating the control allocator can be minimised by enforcing discrete dynamics
and bounds on the inputs.
• The reference is optimal at discrete points.
• The optimality of the reference can be defined to induce desirable characteristics, such as high
distance to banks or minimal fuel consumption.
Throughout this section, a pose at time tk is denoted by ηk, and a route is understood as a sequence of
poses, i.e. η0, . . . ηK for some number of steps K. For dynamical purposes, a BODY oriented velocity
over ground vector, νk, is assigned to each pose. Furthermore, uk ∈R5 contains the thrusters RPM
values and the azipod angles applied at pose k, and f(ηk, νk, uk) ∈R3 denotes the right-hand side of
(5.15) with some modifications introduced later.
6.2.2.1
Variable Normalisation and Model Modifications
Formulating an optimisation problem presents its own challenges, especially when dimensional variables
such as ηk, νk and uk are involved. These have different units, and their varying magnitudes can degrade
the eﬀiciency and reliability of most gradient-based algorithms. This degradation can be mitigated
by normalising the variables to be dimensionless. Formulating an objective also becomes easier since
weights can be chosen without consideration for the scales of different units. Motivated by these benefits,
ηk, νk and uk will be normalised in implementation. For this purpose, let there be known bounds such
that
ηlb ≤ηk ≤ηub,
(6.9)
νlb ≤νk ≤νub,
(6.10)
ulb ≤uk ≤uub,
(6.11)
==PAGE:71==
6.2 Model Based Optimal Routing
64
for all discrete time steps k ∈N. From these, scaling matrices, S, and translation vectors, T , can be
obtained as
Sη = diag [ηub −ηlb]−1 ,
Tη = −ηlb,
(6.12)
Sν = diag [νub −νlb]−1 ,
Tν = −νlb,
(6.13)
Su = diag [uub −ulb]−1 ,
Tu = −ulb.
(6.14)
Then, the transformations from dimensional to normalised variables are
ˆηk = Sη (ηk + Tη) ∈[0, 1],
ˆνk = Sν (νk + Tν) ∈[0, 1],
ˆuk = Su (uk + Tu) ∈[0, 1].
Using normalised variables is essential in implementation but often leads to complicated expressions
that are hard to interpret. The optimisation problem will here be expressed using dimensional variables
to keep the notation simple.
Consider the terms in (5.18) on the form
|x|y
(6.15)
where x and y may be any state or input combination. As mentioned, eﬀicient optimisation solvers are
gradient-based, requiring the objective and all constraints to be differentiable. However, when x ̸= y,
the terms (6.15) are non-differentiable with respect to x at x = 0. To ensure differentiability, the terms
with x ̸= y are substituted with the smooth approximation
x tanh(ζx)y, ζ > 1.
(6.16)
The terms with x = y are differentiable, but their derivatives vanish at x = 0. This is problematic
for input terms, e.g. |nbow1|nbow1. To illustrate, suppose the bow thrusters are deactivated, nbow1 =
nbow2 = 0, so the corresponding derivatives vanish. From the algorithm’s perspective, reactivating the
thrusters will not affect the system dynamics, only increase the objective. Consequently, they remain
deactivated indefinitely, significantly reducing the ships’ manoeuvrability. In order to prevent this, the
input terms on the form
|nx|nx
are replaced with the piecewise function

k=0 κ(uk) - penalises use of and changes in input.
The equality constraints (6.20)-(6.21) enforce the discrete dynamics (5.18) with the modification de-
scribed above.
The inequality constraints (6.22)-(6.24) enforce the bounds (6.9)-(6.11), and (6.26)
ensure the ship does not collide with the outline at any step, so all poses are contained within the
obstacle free region, N free. It is assumed that a fixed initial condition, (η0, ν0), is provided. Note that
(6.19) is minimised with respect to the velocity over the ground, ν, as opposed to relative velocity, νr.
When a varying current is present, the relative velocity becomes a function of the ship’s position, which
would have required additional constraints. Currents are instead managed in the dynamic constraints
(6.21). The scales 1/K ensure the sums in the objective are bounded independently of the number of
steps, K. Without the scales, the sums would dominate over the terminal condition, ρ, given enough
steps. The function ρ in the objective is on the form
ρ(ηK, νK) = ˜ηT
KSη ˜ηK + ˜νT
KSν ˜νK,
where Sη and Sv are positive semi-definite weight matrices, and
˜ηK = ηK −ηK,
˜νK = νK −νK,
denotes the terminal states’ deviation from some desired states ηK and νK. The function ℓpenalises
bank distances:
ℓ(ηk, νk) = C
N
N
X
i=1
exp [β(1 −αi(ηk))] ,
(6.27)
where C > 0 is a scalar weight and β is a constant chosen as
β = −ln(10)
1 −αmax
,
such that
exp [β(1 −αmax)] = 1
10,
for some αmax > 1. Intuitively, ℓpenalises small scaling coeﬀicients exponentially more than larger ones,
and αmax represents a maximum scaling coeﬀicient of interest. That is, any α > αmax corresponds to
triangles so far away that they should be ignored in the optimisation process. The κ-function is on the
form
κ(uk) = κin(uk) + κmane(uk),
where κin(uk) and κmane(uk) are respectively an input and a manoeuvrability term. The input term,
κin(uk), is given by
κin(uk) = uT
k P uk +
 ∆ukPd∆uk

if k = 0
(6.28)
where P and Pd are positive semi-definite weight matrices. Here, uT

change. The maneuverability term, κmane(uk), is
κmane(uk) = M (fN (cos(α1,k)) + fN (cos(α2,k))) ,
(6.29)
where (α1,k, α2,k) are the azipods angles at the k’th pose, M is a scalar weight and fN (·) is the
probability density function for a normal distribution centred at zero with variance σ2
N . This term
penalises when the azipods are angled approximately perpendicular to the heading. When orientated
as such, the ability to produce surge is significantly reduced, leading to a loss of manoeuvrability.

6.3 Generating Routes
67
6.2.2.3
Continuous Reference Signal
After the optimisation problem is solved, a continuous reference, r, is constructed by interpolating
through the optimal route using a cubic spline. Many control designs rely on higher-order derivatives
of the reference. Hence, continuous first and second-order derivatives of the reference are desirable.
Some spline interpolations, such as MATLAB’s spline function, ensure the second-order derivative is
continuous, but these are not shape-preserving in some cases. The derivatives will instead be constructed
by utilising that the solution, η∗
k, ν∗
k, and u∗
k, to (6.19) obey the discrete dynamics (5.18). As such, the
velocity and acceleration in NED coordinates can be found as
˙η∗
k = R(η∗
k)ν∗
k,
(6.30)
¨η∗
k = ˙R(η∗
k)ν∗
k + R(η∗
k)f(η∗
k, ν∗
k, u∗
k).
(6.31)
Then, ˙rr(t) and ¨r(t) are constructed by interpolating through ˙η∗
k and ¨η∗
k respectively.
6.3
Generating Routes
When departing from Gedser Harbour, the ship must perform complex manoeuvres in a constrained
environment. As such, a good reference trajectory is essential to achieve satisfactory closed-loop be-
haviour. For this purpose, an initial route is generated using the RRT method, which thereafter is used
to initiate the MBOR optimisation problem.
6.3.1
Rapidly-Exploring Random Tree
The RRT-based methods described in Section 6.1 will now be considered for route generation. Trees
will be implemented using the MATLAB class MATLAB-tree [Tin22]. The following will be used as
the initial and terminal state,
η0 =


390
190
−20

,
ηN =


−600
450
−190

,
(6.32)
which corresponds to beginning at the top of the harbour facing north and ending at the bottom of the
harbour facing south. The terminal state will be added to the tree if the tree is within the connection
distance, d, of the terminal state. If not, a route will be linearly interpolated towards the terminal state
with steps of length at most d. This could potentially violate the system dynamics in (6.1) and will
be discussed in Section 6.3.1.3. The generated route will be used as an initial condition for the MBOR
approach, which will then create the continuous reference consisting of position and heading along with
their first and second order derivatives. Note that the RRT-based methods themselves do not admit a
time instance for each pose. Hence the plots in following RRT results are just an illustration where the
time between consecutive poses is chosen as Ts = 8 seconds.
6.3.1.1
Computing Control Action
Both RRT methods assume that control actions, u1, u2, can be found which moves the system (6.1)
towards the randomly sampled points. For simplicity, a grid search has been chosen with a resolution
of 50 points per dimension to compute the control actions. The grid search algorithm returns a feasible
point with the lowest line cost among the searched points. The weight matrix of the cost function is
given by,
Q =


1
0
0
0
1
0
0
0
50

.
(6.33)
This ensures that difference in the heading is weighted more than the (North,East)-position. These
specific values gave good results in testing. In general, a higher weight on the heading gives straighter
paths.
==PAGE:75==
6.3 Generating Routes
68
6.3.1.2
Sample Space Creation
From theory the sample space N free needs to be sampled uniformly. N free is diﬀicult to model as the
valid headings depend heavily on the position of the ship and hence is not easily predictable. Thus,
this approach is deemed impractical. Instead, it will be estimated. The estimation of the sample space
consists of two parts.
First a set of valid (North,East)-positions is created.
For each of the valid
positions, accompanying valid headings are needed. This is done by simply running through a fixed
amount of trial headings and selecting the valid ones. As there are a lot more points in the centre
of the harbour, the points with (North,East)-positions in the top and in the bottom of the harbour
are duplicated. Experiments show that this makes the RRT algorithm converge towards a valid route
with fewer iterations. Before this duplication, the RRT algorithm would often need 20, 000 to 30, 000
iterations to converge, while approximately 5, 000 to 10, 000 are needed presently. This illustrates the
importance of uniform sampling in sampling based methods.
6.3.1.3
Route Generation and Testing
The generation of a route using each RRT method along with different tests are now considered. The
specifics of the runs can be seen in Table 6.1. From Figure 6.6 it can be seen that the methods are
successful in generating routes going from the top of Gedser Harbour to the bottom with a turning
manoeuvre in between. The main difference between the RRT and RRT* methods is that the RRT*
algorithm has straightened the route a small amount.
This is the most evident for the heading in
Figure 6.7(b) where several bumps have been removed at time points close to t = 50 s and t = 200 s.
The results of increasing the connection distance, d, even further can be seen in Figure 6.8(a). Here the
larger bumps in heading close to t = 50 and t = 200 have been removed entirely as well as some minor
adjustments in the (North,East)-positions. This behaviour is expected as RRT* aims to find shorter
connections between nodes in the tree within a radius of d.

Table 6.1: The different experiments performed using the RRT-based methods.
One limitation of the choice of connection distance, d, can be seen in Figure 6.8(b). Here a jump is
seen at around (N, E) = (0, 450). It violates the ship dynamics as it connects points that are close in
position and heading but not close in the sense of ship dynamics. Close in the sense of ship dynamics
means that there exists a feasible and short input sequence between them. This is an error in the
implementation as the algorithm itself should take care of this in the IS_VALID function, which it does
not. This error was found late in the writing of the thesis and hence it was deemed unimportant as the
behaviour was found to be satisfactory and MBOR would correct any infeasibility in model dynamics.
Similarly, the use of linear interpolation from the tree towards the terminal condition could potentially
violate the ship dynamics. The interpolation itself was done to ensure that the terminal condition would
always be a node in the tree. This was also disregarded as only a few nodes would be added and MBOR
would correct any infeasibility in the model dynamics.
The 10, 000 iterations used in a lot of the experiments take a lot of computational effort.
The
methods can be used with far fewer iterations. Already at 2600 iterations, the methods produce very
similar routes to the ones generated in Figure 6.6, which is almost a quarter of the iterations. Especially
for the RRT* method, this makes a large difference in computational effort and time, since the method
performs tree refinement in each iteration.
Without the use of interpolation, the RRT methods do not always converge to a route - and if they
do, it can take many iterations. One way to bias the methods towards converging would be using goal

biasing. To this end, a goal region was created, which consisted of the fairway south of N = −500.
Sampling 30% of the time in the goal region resulted in a route in 800 iterations for both RRT and
RRT*. In comparison, both methods needed 2600 iterations to generate a comparable route without
goal biasing. Hence goal biasing results in almost a third of the iterations for the particular seed chosen
in the run.

6.3 Generating Routes
71
6.3.2
Generating Optimal Routes with MBOR
Generating routes with MBOR is more involved compared to the RRT approach; there are more pa-
rameters to tune, and complex gradients must be implemented to ensure eﬀiciency. In return, MBOR
provides smooth references that can easily be adjusted to account for external forces or limitations in
the control system. Two routes will be generated as a demonstration. The first will allow adjustments to
the parameters to hopefully make the second route more suited for control purposes. All input related
vectors throughout this section will be on the form
u = [nazi1, nazi2, α1, α2, nbow]T ,
where nbow is the rpm of both bow thrusters, i.e. the two bow thrusters are actuated together as a
single unit. The bow thrusters are modelled as such throughout the rest of the thesis.
The first step in the setup process is to specify the bounds (6.9)-(6.11). For this demonstration:

where h is the time in seconds between sequential poses. The route duration is the product between
h and the number of steps, K. Hence, choosing a large h means fewer steps (and thereby also fewer
optimisation variables) are required to generate a route of a certain duration.
On the other hand,
choosing a small h improves the accuracy of the Euler integration (6.20)-(6.21). Therefore, h is a balance
between computational eﬀiciency and dynamical accuracy. In this demonstration, h = 2 seconds and
K = 291, so the route duration is

or just under ten minutes. The objective parameters are set to the values in Table 6.2. These must be

using the azipod thrusters. Instead, a specialised constraint is added:

The problem (6.19) is a large nonlinear optimisation
problem, and requires a solver designed for this purpose to be solved eﬀiciently. Here, MATLAB’s
fmincon solver and its interior point implementation will be used. This solver requires an initial guess
of the optimal route. The trivial choice is to set all poses equal to the initial conditions, η0 and ν0,
but this leads to slow convergence since the solver must implement a turning manoeuvre on its own.
One way to give the algorithm a headstart is to use an initial route with a turning manoeuvre. The
RRT route in Figure 6.6(b) constitutes a suitable choice, but it only consists of 79 poses and must be
extended to K + 1 poses (initial condition included). To this end, let ηRRT

the objective. For this route, MBOR exploits that the bow thrusters’ performance at higher velocities
is overestimated to produce sway throughout the route. Outside the turning manoeuvre (before 200
seconds and after 400 seconds), the sway is so high relative to the surge velocity that it cannot be
produced in practice. Hence, this route will be referred to as the high sway route. In an attempt to
improve the feasibility of tracking the route, a second route with lower sway at higher surge velocities
will be generated. From testing, it was found that

ure 6.11(b) and Figure 6.12(b). This route will be referred to as the variable sway route. Comparing
the velocities in Figure 6.11, the variable sway route has much lower sway when the ship is in forward
motion and appears easier to track than the high sway route. However, it will be evident in control
==PAGE:80==
6.3 Generating Routes

testing that the high sway route is actually the easiest to track. The problem with the variable sway
route is that the sway during the turning manoeuvre is too low relative to the yaw velocity. Based on
experience from the simulator, achieving the yaw velocity in Figure 6.11(b) results in sway higher than 1
m/s in magnitude, causing an overshoot when tracking. The overshoot is also present when tracking the
high sway route, but it is less pronounced due to the route’s higher sway during the turning manoeuvre.
The identified model also underestimates the bow thrusters’ performance at lower velocities, causing
the low sway observed in the variable sway route. As is, the model inaccuracies are too significant for
reliable reference generation that the actual ship would be able to follow in both position and velocity.
This can be further illustrated by comparing the variable sway route with a route performed by the
real ship; see Figure 6.10(c). The real ship performs the turning manoeuvre with a lower turn rate.
Furthermore, at one point during the turning manoeuvre, the real ship has zero downward velocity in
NED coordinates. Based on the ship’s orientation at this point, the low downward velocity may be
attributed to low sway velocity. That is, the real ship performs the turning manoeuvre with lower sway
and yaw velocities than observed in the routes generated by MBOR. Note that this is only one of many
actual transits, and the route performed varies depending on the transit considered. Despite the veloc-
ity mismatches, alternative references, such as using the actual data, proved less reliable when tracking.
The routes generated using MBOR will therefore be used going forward. Besides the difference in sway,
the high sway and variable sway routes are similar with minor differences. One difference is that the
high sway route reaches the fairway faster, with lower fuel consumption as seen in Table 6.3.
Recall that the dynamics (5.18) were modified to make them suitable for optimisation. The accumu-
lation errors of these modifications are quantified in Figure 6.13 with the absolute errors between the
high sway route and an unmodified open-loop simulation. The open-loop simulation was done using
Euler integration with the same step-size to isolate the modification errors. There are spikes in all veloc-
ity errors, but these are far from significant, and the final position errors are measured in centimetres.
Note that the ending growth in east error can be attenuated by increasing ζ in (6.16). Considering
these observations, the accumulation error is deemed a non-issue.
Finally, a continuous reference signal along with first and second-order derivatives are constructed
from the high sway and variable sway routes. The high sway route’s reference signal and its derivatives
are visualised in Figure 6.14. A corresponding figure for the variable sway route is included in Figure A.1

route. Note that the velocities and accelerations are in NED coordinates.
6.4
Discussion
The choice of model and accompanying controller for the RRT method has a large impact on the way
it performs. The simple nature of the model and the discrete nature of the grid search are the most
probable causes for the erratic behaviour of the generated routes. Choosing a new model or designing a
controller for the specific model used in the RRT approach could lead to much smoother results. This
would open for the possibility of using an interpolated RRT route directly as a reference.
MBOR is not restricted to the dynamics identified in this thesis. The dynamics can be changed to
model other ships by changing the input variables, uk, and the system function, f, in (6.21). There are
some requirements that must be satisfied for reliability and eﬀiciency. First, the system function should
be differentiable, as otherwise eﬀicient gradient-based solvers cannot be applied.
This may sound
restrictive, but many non-differentiable terms can be adequately approximated using differentiable
functions. Furthermore, the system function should be fast to evaluate, and its gradient should be
==PAGE:84==
6.4 Discussion
77
simple enough that it can be implemented.
If the gradient cannot be implemented, the standard
procedure is to estimate it using finite differences, which can become computationally expensive.
MBOR is best suited for guidance in constrained environments where complex manoeuvres are
necessary. Less complex methods become viable when the ship is supposed to sail forward with soft
turns, e.g. Dubins paths [Fos21].
An addition to MBOR that could be considered in the future would be including data about depths.
Using the electronic chart, approximate depth information could be used in planning.
This would
remove the hard boundary in the turning basin, which is used at present.
For the particular area
around Gedser harbour, the addition likely wouldn’t have a significant impact. Minor adjustments
would be possible in the turning basin as it would not be heavily penalised by getting near the edge.
Another approach would be trying to sail in areas where most of the ship traﬀic already resides. These
areas would be assumed to be best practices, i.e. optimal in some sense. Similar to the approach used
in [EG21], one could estimate a probability density function of routes taken by other ships. Generated
routes outside of this area should be penalised as to encourage routes within the area. In this way,
implicit knowledge from many navigators could be captured by the autonomous guidance system.
==PAGE:85==
CHAPTER 7
Virtual Inputs and Control
Allocation
The identified model dynamics in (5.15) are not suitable for control design, particularly because there
is not a one-to-one correspondence between a desired actuation on the system and the available inputs.
Section 7.1 simplifies the design process by replacing all input related terms in the identified model
with virtual inputs. Thereafter, Sections 7.2-7.3 present three allocation strategies: dynamic allocation,
singularity avoidance allocation and predictive allocation.
The first is not applicable but serves as
motivation for the subsequent two. Finally, the chapter ends with a comparison between the singularity
avoidance and the predictive allocation strategies. An overview of the control allocation can be seen in
Figure 7.1.

Control design benefits from simple models with input terms separate from the unperturbed system

Formulating the input terms as above is inspired by [Fos21]. Notice the rudder and main propeller have

Ignoring physical limitations, infinitely many input combinations can produce a desired actuation
on the system. Hence, g(·) is not a bijective mapping, which complicates most control designs. To
simplify the design process, the input terms are substituted with virtual inputs, leading to the system
˙η = R(η)(νr + νc),
(7.6)

(7.7)
Given τctrl from the control law, the control forces and azipod angles must be distributed such that
τctrl = T (α)fn.
Allocating fn and α can be challenging, especially when physical limitations must be accounted for.
The following sections present ways of allocating these variables.
The virtual inputs, τctrl, will be
denoted as τ throughout this chapter to simplify notation.

7.2 Dynamic Allocation

7.2
Dynamic Allocation
Dynamic allocation finds appropriate values for fn and α by determining dynamic allocation laws, ˙fn
and ˙α, such that the currently produced virtual inputs converge to some desired values. To elaborate,
let
˜τ = τ −T (α)fn,
(7.8)
be the difference between a reference virtual input, τ, and the currently produced virtual input. More-
over, consider its time derivative given by
˙˜τ = ˙τ −M(α, fn) ˙α −T (α) ˙fn,
(7.9)
where
M(α, fn) =


−a7 sin(α1)fazi1
−a7 sin(α2)fazi2
b10 cos (α1) fazi1 −b11 sin (α1) fazi1
b10 cos (α2) fazi2 + b11 sin (α2) fazi2
c10 cos (α1) fazi1 −c11 sin (α1) fazi1
c10 cos (α2) fazi2 + c11 sin (α2) fazi2


is the Jacobian matrix of T (α) fn with respect to α.
Then, allocation laws,
˙fn and ˙α, must be
determined such that ˜τ converges to zero. Here, allocation laws will be derived using a sliding mode
approach. To this end, collect the allocation laws in
˙z =

˙α
˙fn

,
such that (7.9) can be written on the form
˙˜τ = ˙τ −[M(α, fn), T (α)]
|

˙z.
(7.10)
As a controllability criterion, assume that P has full row rank, such that a right inverse, P +, exists.
Choosing
˙z = P +(α, fn)ξ,
where ξ ∈R3 can be considered an input, further simplifies (7.10) to
˙˜τ = ˙τ −ξ.
(7.11)
If bounds exist such that
| ˙τi| ≤∆i for all t,
then setting
ξi = (∆i + ki) sign(˜τi)
with ki > 0 ensures finite time convergence of ˜τ to zero; see Corollary 1. In terms of α and fn, the
allocation law is

˙α
˙fn

= P +(α, fn)Λ sign(˜τ)
(7.12)
where Λ is a diagonal matrix with
Λi,i = (∆i + ki) for i = 1, 2, 3.
The scheme (7.12) is simple and computationally eﬀicient. Additionally, the controller and allocator
combination becomes a cascaded dynamical system, simplifying efforts to prove closed-loop stability
compared to the subsequent allocators. Unfortunately, (7.12) will often exceed the capabilities of the
thrusters. [Gri+10] presents a similar approach that can incorporate bounds on fn and α. However,
==PAGE:88==
7.3 Optimal Allocation
81
their results assume a feasible solution exists within these bounds. This is often not the case, even if
the virtual inputs are within the thrusters’ capabilities. For example, suppose the azipods are angled
towards aft, and the virtual inputs require positive surge acceleration. In this scenario, fulfilling the
virtual inputs would require an unrealistic high turn-rate of the azipods. More generally, the virtual
input may be infeasibly large or require some infeasible combination, e.g. high surge and sway at the
same time. Consequently, a dynamic approach is unsuitable for the case considered, and an approach
that can handle inconsistencies is required.
7.3
Optimal Allocation
Optimisation-based approaches allocate fn and α by solving an optimisation problem. This is more
computationally intensive than dynamic allocation but offers increased flexibility. In particular, incon-
sistencies can be handled with the inclusion of slack variables, s ∈R3. Henceforth, the objective is thus
to solve
s + τ = T (α)fn
(7.13)
with s being zero when possible, and otherwise, the objective is to find an optimal compromise between
input usage and minimisation of slack in surge, sway and yaw. Solutions must be achievable within a
sample duration, h, i.e. n and α must satisfy the following constraints:
nmin ≤n ≤nmax,
(7.14)
∆nmin ≤n −n0 ≤∆nmax,
(7.15)
∆αmin ≤α −α0 ≤∆αmax,
(7.16)
where n0 and α0 are the values of the thrusters’ RPM and the azipods’ angles before allocation. Hence,
(7.15)-(7.16) limit the input change allowed during one sample duration. The bounds in (7.15)-(7.16)
depend on the ship’s manoeuvrability, agility and the sampling time. For instance, if a control action
is sampled every two seconds, and the azipods can turn at most 9 deg/s, then
−∆αmin,i = ∆αmax,i = 2 s · 9 deg/s = 18 deg for i = 1, 2.
Constraints on fn can be derived using that frpm is strictly increasing with respect to n. As such,
frpm(nmin) ≤frpm(n) ≤frpm(nmax),
and
frpm(n0 + ∆nmin) ≤frpm(n) ≤frpm(n0 + ∆nmax).
Combining these, it is seen that fn = frpm(n) must satisfy
fn,min ≤fn ≤fn,max,
(7.17)
where
fn,min = max [frpm(nmin), frpm(n0 + ∆nmin)] ,
(7.18)
fn,max = min [frpm(nmax), frpm(n0 + ∆nmax)] .
(7.19)
Two approaches to solve (7.13) will be presented: one inspired by [JFB04], and an extension incorpo-
rating elements from model predictive control.
==PAGE:89==
7.3 Optimal Allocation
82
7.3.1
Allocation with Singularity Avoidance
[JFB04] defines rank-deficient T (α) as a singular configuration. For a fully or over-actuated system,
full rank T (α) means force can be produced in every direction.
Consequently, when T (α) is near
rank-deficient, the ability to produce force in at least one direction is reduced, leading to a loss of ma-
noeuvrability. This can only occur when the azipods are angled in such a way that no forward propulsion
can be produced from T (α). As such, regaining manoeuvrability requires turning the azipods, which
can take several seconds. During this time, the ship is vulnerable to external forces, reducing robustness
of the closed-loop system. Therefore, from a robustness perspective, avoiding singular configurations is
desirable. [JFB04] presents an optimal allocation strategy incorporating singularity avoidance. Here, a
slight modification will be applied to solve (7.13).
[JFB04] suggests a nonlinear optimisation problem similar to:
min
fn,α,s
n
f T
n P fn + (fn −fn,0)T D (fn −fn,0) + sT Qs + (α −α0)T Ω(α −α0)
+
ϱ
ε + det (T (α)W T T (α))


s.t.
s + τ = T (α)fn
(7.21)
fn,min ≤fn ≤fn,max
(7.22)
∆αmin ≤α −α0 ≤∆αmax
(7.23)
Here, fn,0 = frpm(n0), and the constraints (7.21)-(7.23) enforce (7.13), (7.17) and (7.16), respectively.
P , D, Q and Ωare positive semi-definite weight matrices:
P - penalises control forces and replaces a non-quadratic power consumption term used in [JFB04].
D - penalises control force changes and serves as chattering attenuation.
Ω- penalises slack and should be so large that s ≈0 whenever possible.
Q - penalises azipod angle changes.
The final term penalises the singularity of T (α), with

Similar to the MBOR approach, fn and α are normalised in implementation to improve reliabil-
ity. The slack variables are effectively unbounded and cannot be normalised. Instead, their typical
magnitudes must be considered when choosing Q; one suggestion is given in Section 7.4.
[JFB04] suggests solving a quadratic approximation instead of (7.20). This improves eﬀiciency but
assumes, among other things, that T (α) can be linearly approximated for α satisfying (7.16). [JFB04]
can justify this assumption, as they only consider azipods with a turn rate of 1 deg/s. In contrast, M/F
Copenhagen’s azipods can achieve a turn rate of 9 deg/s, making the justification more complicated.
Nevertheless, a quadratic approximation was implemented but performed poorly compared to solving
(7.20) directly and will not be considered further.
7.3.2
Predictive Allocation
One weakness of the singularity avoidance allocator, (7.20), is that it favours shortsighted strategies
that can be achieved in a single sample duration.
For example, suppose the virtual inputs can be
fulfilled in two ways: either fully by rotating the azipods over multiple sample durations, or partially
in a single sample duration by activating the bow thrusters. Given these two strategies, (7.20) will
often favour the latter to minimise immediate slack, even when this leads to higher fuel consumption.
Similar scenarios are common, and (7.20) generally uses the bow thrusters more than the captains. The
shortsighted behaviour can be improved by incorporating elements from model predictive control; see
e.g. [Han+11]. Essentially, the virtual inputs are not fulfilled immediately but over the duration of Nh
samples, referred to as the allocation horizon. This improves the method’s foresight since future actions
can be considered in the allocation process. Given τ from the control law, the predictive problem is to
find an optimal solution to
si + τ = T (αi)fn,i for i = 1, . . . , Nh,
(7.25)
where si, fn,i, αi are slack, control forces and azipod angles in the i’th sample duration. This leads
to predictions of future rpm values, n1, . . . , nNh, and azipod angles, α1, . . . , αNh. Of these, only n1
and α1 are actually implemented. The rest are planned control actions that are adapted if τ changes
in later samples. Due to its predictive nature, this allocation strategy will be referred to as predictive
allocation.
The predictive strategy allocates by solving the optimisation problem:
min
n1,...,nNh,
α1,...,αNh,
s1,...,sNh
Nh
X
i=1

nT
i P ni + (ni −ni−1)T D (ni −ni−1)
+wisT
i Qsi + (αi −αi−1)T Ω(αi −αi−1)

(7.26)
s.t.
si + τ = T (αi) ˆfrpm(ni)
for i = 1 . . . Nh
(7.27)
nmin ≤ni ≤nmax
for i = 1 . . . Nh
(7.28)
∆nmin ≤ni −ni−1 ≤∆nmax
for i = 1 . . . Nh
(7.29)
∆αmin ≤αi −αi−1 ≤∆αmax
for i = 1 . . . Nh
(7.30)
Formulating the problem in terms of rpm values was necessary to bound the changes from ni−1 to ni.
Achieving the same whilst using control forces would have required constraints on the form
∆nmin ≤f −1

However, these constraints are unsuitable for gradient-based optimisation since the derivative of f −1
rpm
is undefined at zero. In contrast, frpm is differentiable everywhere, and so is its approximation, ˆfrpm,
defined in (6.17). Hence, using ˆfrpm in (7.27) maintains differentiability and circumvents the issues
caused by the derivative of frpm vanishing at zero; see Section 6.2.2. The constraint (7.27) approximates
(7.25) and (7.28)-(7.30) ensures all predicted inputs are feasible. The weight matrices in (7.26) serve
the same purpose as in the singularity avoidance allocator, but now penalise throughout the allocation
horizon. The positive scalars wi reflect the importance of fulfilling τ in different sample durations.
These should be strictly decreasing, i.e.
w1 > w2 > . . . > wNh−1 > wNh > 0,
so the predictive allocator prioritises fulfilling τ early.
It is important that Q is not overly large,
otherwise (7.26) reduces to fulfilling τ in the first sample duration, thus favouring shortsighted strategies
similar to the singularity avoidance allocator.
The singularity avoidance term from (7.20) is omitted because it often leads to undesirable charac-
teristics. For instance, (7.20) refuses to rotate the azipods such that they cross the 90 degrees angle
(perpendicular to surge) when ϱ is too large. On the other hand, if ϱ is too small, then the term has
no obvious effect. Finding a balance between the two proved challenging, especially since it was sen-
sitive to external disturbances. Choosing Q extremely large worked but also introduced scaling issues
that decreased the reliability. It is almost inevitable that T (α) becomes singular during the turning
manoeuvre, possibly explaining the issues observed.
Let n∗
i and α∗
i for i = 1 . . . Nh denote the solution to (7.26) in the previous time sample. Then, the
initial guess, n0
i and α0
i for i = 1 . . . Nh, for the current iteration should be
n0

 n∗
i+1
if i < Nh
n∗
i
otherwise
and α0
i =
 α∗
i+1
if i < Nh
α∗
i
otherwise
.
This initial guess allows the predictive allocator to adapt the previously planned inputs. One can use the
current RPM values and azipod angle as an initial guess the first time (7.26) is solved. As with all other
optimisation-based methods, ni and αi are normalised in implementation to improve reliability and
eﬀiciency. Similarly to before, si for i = 1, . . . , Nh are effectively unbounded and cannot be normalised.
7.4
Allocator Comparison
The two allocation strategies presented in Section 7.3 will here be compared to illustrate their main
differences. Of particular interest is how they utilise the thrusters under similar circumstances. Their
performance will be compared using the known dynamics (5.14) without any external disturbances.
These are the best possible circumstances, but experience shows the results do reflect the allocators’
general behaviour. Although FORCE Technology’s simulator would have given more general results, it
would also have made the inference more diﬀicult. For comparison purposes, the predictive allocator
(7.26) will be referred to as PA, and the allocator with singularity avoidance (7.20) will be referred to
as SAA.
The parameters shared by the allocators will be identical. These can be found in Table 7.1 along with

All things considered, PA has more desirable characteristics but is less computationally eﬀicient.

Table 7.2(b). Nevertheless, PA is fast enough and could be even faster if implemented in a lower level
language such as C. Henceforth, PA is preferred over SAA when control systems are tested.
7.5
Discussion
The optimal allocation strategies presented minimise different powers of the RPM values. By minimising
the squared control forces, the singularity avoidance allocator effectively minimises RPM values to the
fourth power. On the other hand, the predictive allocator minimises RPM values to the second power.
==PAGE:94==
7.5 Discussion
87
Higher powers increase the priority on minimising larger values compared to smaller ones. Hence, the
difference in powers may explain some of the behaviour observed in Section 7.4, e.g. PA deactivates the
thrusters while SAA does not. It would have been more correct to use identical powers, particularly
the absolute value of the third power, which is proportional to the energy consumption of the thrusters
[JFB04].
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 8
Control System
The control objective in this thesis is to manoeuvre the ship to track a reference signal, r(t), in the
presence of time-varying and possibly unmeasured disturbance forces such as wind, waves, currents
and unmodelled dynamics. To this end, different designs will be applied and compared to determine
the control design that best achieves the objective. An overview of the control system can be seen in
Figure 8.1. Section 8.1 first linearises the identified model using input-to-state linearisation. Afterwards,
two designs for stabilising the linearised system are presented: full-state feedback PID and sliding
mode. Section 8.2 presents an integrator backstepping design that uses gain matrices defined in BODY
coordinates and relaxes the assumptions made in Section 8.1.
Section 8.3 incorporates the ship’s
capabilities in a linear model predictive design that finds optimal inputs. Section 8.4 separates the
tracking problem into an online guidance system and a velocity control system. Section 8.5 presents a
strategy for smooth transitions between control strategies during traversals. The presented designs are
evaluated and compared in Section 8.7. Finally, Section 8.8 proposes a control solution based on the
results in Section 8.7.
Stability throughout this section is proved using time-invariant Lyapunov theory. Hence, all stability
results will be uniform.
