8.1
Input-to-State Linearisation Control
Linearising a nonlinear system can significantly ease the control design process. Considering this, input-
to-state linearisation is here applied to linearise (7.6)-(7.7). Then, two different designs to stabilise the
linearised system will be presented.
==PAGE:96==
8.1 Input-to-State Linearisation Control
89
8.1.1
Linearising the System
[EL91] defines a nonlinear system to be in controllable canonical form if it is on the form:
˙x1 = x2,
˙x2 = x3,
...
˙xn−1 = xn,
˙xn = f(x1, . . . , xn) + g(x1, . . . , xn)u,
or, equivalently, if the system can be written as a single n′th order differential equation:
x(n) = f

x, ˙x, . . . , x(n−1)
+ g

x, ˙x, . . . , x(n−1)
u.
(8.1)
A system on the form (8.1) can trivially be linearised for invertible g(·) using the input
u = g−1(·) (γ −f (·)) ,
where γ induces desired behaviour in the linearised system
x(n) = γ.
Now let
y = [y1, . . . , yn]T ,
contain the states of an arbitrary n’th order nonlinear system. The idea in input-to-state linearisation
is to find a diffeomorphism:
z = ϕ(y),
such that ˙z is on controllable canonical form. Then, the dynamics ˙z can be linearised similarly to how
(8.1) was linearised above. [EL91] presents conditions for the existence of such a diffeomorphism and,
if it does, how to construct it. However, these are mathematically complex and often unnecessary, as a
suitable diffeomorphism can be found using a strategy illustrated below.
Consider the tracking error
e = η −r,
where r ∈R3 is a reference signal in NED coordinates. The strategy is now to differentiate e with
respect to time until the virtual inputs appear. First differentiation:
˙e = R(η)(νr + νc) −˙r.
(8.2)
Second differentiation:
¨e = ˙R(η)(νr + νc) + R(η) ˙νr −¨r
(8.3)
= ˙R(η)(νr + νc) + R(η) (f(η, νr) + τctrl + d(t)) −¨r.
(8.4)
Here, the time derivative of the rotation matrix, R(·), is
˙R(η) =

One limitation of input-to-state linearisation is that only known dynamics can be cancelled. Therefore,
the unknown disturbance forces, d(t), still influence the linearised system and must be taken into
account when designing γ. [EL91] restricts γ to employ linear control techniques whereas γ may be
nonlinear in this thesis.
8.1.2
PID control
Proportional-Integral-Derivative (PID) control is a well-known control methodology that has seen ap-
plication within many industries. To drive the tracking error to zero, γ may be chosen as
γ = −Kd ˙e −Kpe −Ki
Z t
0
e(ε) dε
where the proportional, Kp, integral, Ki and derivative, Kd, gain matrices are chosen such that the
augmented system:
¨e = −Kd ˙e −Kpe −Kiei,
˙ei = e,
(8.8)
is uniformly globally exponentially stable; see Lemma 1.
The disturbance force, R(η)d(t), can be
rejected by the integrator, ei, if the disturbance is assumed constant. The validity of this assumption
depends on the disturbance considered. For example, a current disturbance may be assumed fairly
constant independent of the ship’s heading.
Non-constant but slowly varying disturbances may be
attenuated by the integrator, e.g. a mean wind disturbance, which can be considered slowly varying if
the ship’s velocity is also slowly varying.
Various techniques exist within linear theory to find suitable eigenvalues. One is Linear Quadratic
Regulation (LQR); it finds an optimal gain matrices as the solution to the optimisation problem:
min
Kd,Kp,Ki lim
t→∞
Z t
0
zT R1z + γT R2γ dϵ

,
where R1 and R2 are weight matrices, and
z =


ei
e
˙e

,
contains the error states. [HJS08] provides conditions on these that ensure uniform global exponential
stability in closed-loop. These are trivially satisfied if R1 and R2 both are positive definite.
The integrator, ei, may accumulate an excessively large error if the inputs become saturated. This
effect is known as integrator windup and can cause instability. One way to mitigate windup is to adjust
the integration state when the inputs saturate. Specifically, let τsat denoted the inputs after saturation,
i.e. τctrl ̸= τsat if the inputs are saturated and τctrl = τsat otherwise. Then, the equation for ˙ei is
extended by including a anti-windup term:
˙ei = e + K(−1)
i
R(η)Kwu (τctrl −τsat) ,
(8.9)
==PAGE:98==
8.1 Input-to-State Linearisation Control
91
where Kwu is a diagonal and positive definite matrix. To interpret the anti-windup term, suppose that
the heading is constant so R(η) is independent of time. The anti-windup term then appears in the full
control law as follows:
τctrl = −Kwu
Z t
0
τctrl −τsat dε + ....
(8.10)
Here, the anti-windup term will decrease the virtual inputs if τctrl > τsat and increase them if τctrl <
τsat. Thus, the anti-windup term effectively bounds τctrl, thereby also ei. A similar effect is achieved
without a constant heading, but the integral (8.10) becomes more complicated.
8.1.3
Sliding Mode Control
This section will present a basic sliding mode controller that will stabilise the system admitted by
the input-to-state linearisation. The general strategy of a sliding mode controller is to discontinuously
dominate disturbance terms in such a way that the system will behave as desired in finite time. The
desired system dynamics is defined as the sliding surface [EL91]
s(e, ˙e) = ˙e + Λe = 0,
where −Λ must be Hurwitz. Many sliding surfaces exist. The one chosen above leads to exponential
convergence of the tracking error when on the sliding surface.
This gain is introduced later.
The
dynamics of the sliding variable, s, must be considered to develop a method that will drive it to zero.
This is given by
˙s = ¨e + Λ ˙e
= γ + Rd(t) + Λ ˙e.
Note that for notational purposes, the explicit dependence of η on R is omitted. Choosing the control
law, γ, as
γ = −Λ ˙e −K sgn(s),
gives the sliding variable dynamics,
˙s = Rd(t) −K sgn(s).
Here K is a gain matrix assumed diagonal and ∥Rd(t)∥2 = ∥d(t)∥2 is assumed bounded with a known
bound. The vector sign function sgn(·) is given by
sgn(s) =

8.2 Integrator Backstepping

Here it is used that ∥Rd∥2 = ∥d∥2 and that K is assumed symmetric and positive definite. Then if K
is chosen such that λmax(K) is larger than the bound on ∥d∥2, the sliding condition in Theorem 4 is
satisfied. This shows s = 0 is globally asymptotically stable and that s converges to 0 in finite time.
When on the sliding surface, the tracking error, e, is exponentially stable as −Λ is assumed Hurwitz.
This implies tracking the reference signal as desired.
An approximation to the sgn(·) function will be used to attenuate the chattering that occurs when
using a sliding mode controller. Several approximations exist, one being the saturation function ap-
proximation. This is given by [EL91]

by
Φ = {s|si ≤ϕi for i = 1, 2, 3} .
Outside the boundary layer defined, (8.12) works identical to ordinary sliding mode. Therefore, s will
reach the boundary layer in finite time and will stay within for all future time. Moreover, when inside
the boundary layer, e will converge within a finite distance of the origin [EL91]. That is, e is uniformly
global ultimate bounded (UGUB) when (8.12) is used instead of the sign function.

and outright cancelling the signal. With this in mind, an integration state was avoided. Instead, the

In simpler terms, the states are uniformly ultimately globally bounded, with ultimate bound dependent
on the disturbances’ maximum magnitude. Furthermore, the ultimate bound converges to zero as the
magnitude of the disturbances does the same. This result also applies when the virtual inputs, τctrl,
are not fulfilled by the allocator. If this occurs, slack, s, will appear in the closed-loop system as a
disturbance force. Then, the above result ensures that failing to allocate the virtual inputs will not lead
to instability, provided that the slack is bounded.
8.3
Linear Model Predictive Control
Model predictive control (MPC) is a control strategy that is based on the minimisation of an objective
function. Usually the objective function is defined in terms of the control horizon, which is the time
frame for which the control system is considered. System dynamics can be included in the objective
function itself or as constraints on the optimisation problem [RMD20].
Model predictive controllers come in linear and nonlinear variants.
Nonlinear model predictive
control was deemed unfeasible as a prototype had performance issues. One simulation in MATLAB
would take approximately 45 minutes deeming tuning of the controller practically impossible. However,
with eﬀicient implementation of the nonlinear model predictive controller, the method might become
viable. This is deemed out of scope for this thesis. Contrary to nonlinear MPC, linear MPC is much
less computationally expensive. Hence with this reasoning, linear model predictive control (LMPC) will
be considered in this section.
For ease of notation, consider again the model in equations (7.1) and (7.2),

(8.32)

S.
The controller scheme itself is given here. At step k do the following:
1. Obtain states, xk|k.
2. Compute the linearisation, Ak and Bk, by using xk|k and uk|k−1.
3. Solve the optimisation problem in equation (8.50) and obtain U.
4. Apply the control input, uk, i.e. the first vector block of U.
On a note, instead of letting the model predictive controller alter the thruster RPMs and azipod
direction directly, one could let the force allocator handle this task as described in Chapter 7. The
model predictive controller would then control the virtual inputs, which the control allocator would
convert to thruster RPMs and azipod directions. This could be easily included in the model predictive
controller by altering the B matrix to
B =
0
I

.
(8.51)
This approach will not be considered in this thesis. It was merely included as it is a trivial extension
that mimics the architecture of the other controllers.
8.4
Guided Motion Control
Guided motion control is a control strategy wherein a separate guidance system and velocity control
system are used in cascade to achieve a motion control objective [Bre10]. The guidance systems task
is to compute proper velocity commands that, if achieved, will successfully solve the motion control
objective at a kinetic level. The velocity control systems task is then to control the vehicle at a kinematic
==PAGE:106==
8.4 Guided Motion Control
99
level to achieve the velocity commands given by the guidance system in a controlled manner that the
vehicle in question can achieve. For the guidance system two architectures will be considered: Constant
Bearing (CB) guidance and Line-Of-Sight (LOS) guidance, both initially presented in the methodology
chapter. For the velocity controller, a backstepping controller similar to the one derived in Section 8.2
will be considered.
8.4.1
Constant Bearing Guidance
Constant Bearing (CB) guidance is a two-point guidance scheme where the goal is for the vessel to
converge to the a target by aligning the relative interceptor-target velocity along the line of sight. See
Figure 8.2. Here, the line of sight is the direct path between the interceptor and the target.
Figure 8.2: Constant bearing guidance. The interceptor approaches the target by aligning the velocity
along the line of sight. [Bre10].
Consider a vessel on a kinematic level moving in an inertial reference frame. Let the vessels be
represented by it’s position p(t) = [x(t), y(t)]T ∈R2, heading ψ(t) ∈[−π, π] and velocity v(t) =
dp(t)
dt
= [ ˙x(t), ˙y(t)]T ∈R2 relative to the inertial reference frame.
The speed of the vessel will be
denoted as
U(t) = ∥v(t)∥2 =
p
˙x(t)2 + ˙y(t)2 ≥0
(8.52)
For U(t) > 0 the course angle of the vessel is given by
χ(t) = atan2( ˙y(t), ˙x(t)) ∈[−π, π].
(8.53)
and the sideslip (drift) angle of the ship is given by
β(t) = ψ(t) −χ(t).
(8.54)
Thereto, consider a target with position pt(t) = [xt(t), yt(t)]T and velocity vt(t) = ˙pt(t) = [ ˙xt(t), ˙yt(t)]T .
Define the LOS vector, ˜p, as the difference in position of the target and vessel:
˜p = pt(t) −p(t).
(8.55)
The kinematic control objective is thus
lim
t→∞˜p(t) = 0.
(8.56)
CB guidance through direct velocity assignment is now considered [BHF08]. This approach can be
seen illustrated in Figure 8.3. For the direct velocity assignment, the desired velocity is given by
vd(t) = vt(t) + va(t),
(8.57)
where va(t) is the approach velocity. Hence, va(t) describes how fast the vessel should approach the
target and it should follow that va(t) = 0 when ˜p(t) = 0 such that vd(t) = vt(t) and the vessel velocity
==PAGE:107==
8.4 Guided Motion Control
100
Figure 8.3: Constant bearing using direct velocity assignment [BHF08].
should thereby follow the target velocity.
For constant bearing guidance, va(t) can then be chosen as [BHF08]
va(t) = Ua,max(t)
˜p(t)

˜p(t)T ˜p(t) + ∆p(t)2 ,
(8.58)
where Ua,max(t) > 0 determines the maximum approach speed to the target and ∆p(t) > 0 determines
how fast the maximum approach speed is achieved based on the distance to the target. This is illustrated
in Figure 8.4. Here Ut(t) = ∥vt(t)∥2 is the target speed. Thus, the vessel can attain a maximum and
Figure 8.4: Desired velocity as a function of positional error in x (or y).
minimum speed of
Umax(t) = Ut(t) + Ua,max(t),
(8.59a)
Umin(t) = Ut(t) −Ua,max(t).
(8.59b)
Lower ∆p will result in a steeper transitional zone between Umax and Umin.

vd(t) = vt(t) + Ua,max(t)

Using vd(t) = [ ˙xd(t), ˙yd(t)]T , the desired course angle, i.e., the orientation of the velocity vector, can
be computed by
χd(t) = atan2( ˙yd(t), ˙xd(t)).
(8.61)

The desired heading angle of the vessel is thus given by
ψd(t) = χd(t) −βd(t),
(8.62)
where βd(t) is the desired drift angle which has yet to be determined and depends on whether the
heading of the vessel can be controlled independently of the velocities of the vessel.
The stability of CB guidance can then be considered. It follows from [Bre10] that choosing the desired
velocity vd according to (8.60) renders the origin of ˜p UGAS and ULES. Furthermore, [Bre10] shows
that by considering the combined closed-loop position and velocity error system given the nonlinear
time-varying cascade
Σ ˜p : ˙˜p = −Ua,max

In order to apply CB guided motion control to the manoeuvring problem considered in this project,
one can view the reference route r, described in Chapter 6, as a virtual target for the CB guidance law.
The objective then becomes to rendezvous with and follow the virtual target given by the reference
route defined by r(t), ˙r(t), ¨r(t) at time t. Note, for a given time instance the CB guidance only utilises
instantaneous information of the virtual target, i.e., past and future points on the reference route are
not considered, even though these are available since the entirety of r(t) is generated offline. [BSF06]
presents CB guided motion control architectures in which the full path of the target is known and
can thereby utilised; Not only the instantaneous information is used. This control architecture ensures
that the vessel will follow the path of the target, and travel along this path converging to the target.
This type of guided motion control scheme is not considered in this project, but should be readily
implementable by combining the CB guidance already discussed and a variation of the LOS guidance
presented in Section 8.4.2.
8.4.1.2
CB Considerations
For a fully actuated vessel the control vector τctrl is given by
τctrl =


τu
τv
τr

,
(8.72)
while for an underactuated vessel only able to control in surge and yaw, the control vector is given by
τctrl =


τu
0
τr

.
(8.73)
As previously discussed, the estimated system model overestimates the effectiveness of the bow
thrusters and thus the amount of sway the ship is able to produce at high speeds. At low speeds the
bow thrusters are fully effective, thus the ship can be seen as fully actuated and is almost able to
produce control forces in each dimension independently. At high speeds the effectiveness of the bow
==PAGE:110==
8.4 Guided Motion Control
103
thrusters decrease rapidly and the ship becomes for practical purposes underactuated, only being able
to produce control forces in surge and yaw.
Inspired by [BF05], one can construct a variable σ(U) which depends on the ship speed U(t), such that
σ = 1 indicates a fully actuated vessel when at low speeds, and σ = 0 indicates an underactuated vessel
when at high speeds:
σ(U) = 1 −1
2
 
tanh
 
U(t) −Uf +Uu
2
∆σ
!
+ 1
!
.
(8.74)
Here U(t) is the vessel speed, Uf corresponds to the speed at which the ship is fully actuated and Uu
corresponds to the speed at which the ship is underactuated. The zone between Uf and Uu then gives
a transitional speed zone between fully actuated and underactuated. An illustration of σ(U) can be
seen in Figure 8.5. The parameter ∆σ then controls the steepness of this transitional zone. Using σ,
Figure 8.5: σ in (8.74) as a function of the vessel speed U for different values of ∆σ.
the control force in sway τv can be represented by
τv = στv,f + (1 −σ)τv,u,
(8.75)
where τv,f is the control force in the fully actuated case and τv,u = 0 is the control force in the
underactuated case. Note that, given a desired velocity the control force is computed by the kinetic
controller. As such, the desired sway velocity fed to the controller need to reflect that the ship is fully
or underactuated, i.e.,
αv = σαv,f + (1 −σ)αv,u.
(8.76)
αv,f represents the virtual input for the fully actuated case and αv,u represent the virtual input for the
underactuated case, such that τv = τv,f when αv = αv,f and τv = τv,u when αv = αv,u. Considering
the control law (8.69), this can be achieved by choosing αv,f = vd and choosing αv,u such that τv = 0
given by (8.69) when underactuated.
As mentioned earlier the desired course and heading angle are given by (8.61) - (8.62). For the
underactuated case the ship can only be controlled using surge and yaw and thereby ψd(t) = χd(t) such
that βd(t) = 0, while for the fully actuated case the ship heading can be controlled independently of
the course, i.e., βd(t) can be chosen freely. Using the same idea as for τv in (8.75), the desired sideslip
angle can be represented by
βd(t) = σβd,f(t) + (1 −σ)βd,u(t),
(8.77)
where βd,f(t) is the desired sideslip angle for the fully actuated case and βd,u(t) = 0 is the desired
sideslip angle for the underactuated case. For the fully actuated case the ideal sideslip angle would be
==PAGE:111==
8.4 Guided Motion Control
104
βd,f(t) = βt(t) where βt(t) is the targets sideslip angle such that the vessel is able to rendezvous with
the target in both position and heading when fully actuated.
Considering Figure 8.4 and (8.59), if an overshoot in position occurs (˜x < 0), it is more desirable
to have the vessel slow down in order for the target to catch up, instead of reversing or stopping
completely and reaching the target that way. It is thus desired to have Umin(t) > 0 which is ensured if
Ua,max(t) < Ut(t) at all times. One way of doing this is by choosing
Ua,max = ζUt(t),
0 < ζ < 1
(8.78)
such that Umin(t) > 0 and Umax(t) = (1 + ζ)Ut(t). Though, this assumes that the vessel can obtain a
speed higher than the target speed at all times.
Finally, consider the desired heading given by
ψd(t) = χd(t) −βd(t),
(8.79)
with βd(t) as given in (8.77). When underactuated, βd = 0, the desired heading will be assigned the
desired course angle. This is not well posed if it is desired for the ship to sail backwards/reverse, i.e.,
steering with the aft as opposed to the bow. This is the case when the ship needs to reverse out of the
harbour at the start of the route. A simple solution is to compute ψd by ψd = χd −180◦when reversing
is desired. When fully actuated, βd = βt, the reversing should already have been accounted for in the
targets sideslip angle, βt.
8.4.2
Line-Of-Sight Guided Motion Control
Again, consider the interceptor/vessel on a kinematic level as in the previous CB section. Thereto,
consider a path made of straight-line segments given by the lines connecting successive waypoints
pk = [xk, yk]T for k ∈[0, 1, . . . , K].
The task is then to follow the the path.
This is known as a
path-following problem [Fos21], where the task has no temporal constraints and only seeks to fulfil
the spacial constraint of following the path and is therefore considered a spatial task, as opposed to a
temporal task.
Now, consider a path-fixed reference frame with origin pk, where the x-axis has been rotated a
positive angle of αk = atan2(yk+1 −yk, xk+1 −xk) relative to the inertial reference frame. The position
of the vessel relative to the path-fixed reference frame, ε(t) = [s(t), e(t)]T , can then be computed by
ε(t) =

cos αk
−sin αk
sin αk
cos αk
T
(p(t) −pk).
(8.80)
Here, s(t) denotes the along-track distance which is tangential to the path and e(t) denotes the cross-
track error which is normal to the path, see Figure 8.6. Expanding (8.80) yields
s(t) = (x(t) −xk) cos αk + (y(t) −yk) sin αk
(8.81)
e(t) = −(x(t) −xk) sin αk + (y(t) −yk) cos αk
(8.82)
From a path-following perspective the control objective is then to design a kinematic controller such
that
lim
t→∞e(t) = 0,
(8.83)
which ensures that the vessel converges to the path.
Two different LOS guidance principles exists to steer along the LOS vector between two waypoints
while ensuring e(t) →0: Enclosure-based LOS steering and lookahead-based LOS steering.
Only
lookahead-based steering will be considered in this project, as enclosure-based steering corresponds to
a lookahead-based steering scheme with specific chosen parameters [Fos21].
==PAGE:112==
8.4 Guided Motion Control
105
Figure 8.6: LOS guidance course angle assignment for enclosure-based and lookahead-based steering
[Bre10].
For course autopilots, the traditional lookahead-based LOS steering law computes the desired course
angle, χd, by [Fos21]
χd(t) = αk + χr(t),
(8.84a)
χr(t) = −tan−1
 e(t)
∆(t)

,
(8.84b)
where ∆(t) > 0 is the lookahead distance, i.e., how far ahead, along the path, the vessel will look and
steer towards; see Figure 8.6. For heading autopilots, where the heading is used to steer, the thereto
desired heading, ψd, is given by
ψd = χd −βc,
(8.85)
where βc is the sideslip angle caused by environmental forces on the vessel producing a non-zero sway
velocity. The angle χr(t) in (8.84) is denoted the LOS angle and corresponds to the angle between path
and the line of sight from the vessel to the point lying ∆ahead of the vessel along the path. Hence, a
small ∆means more aggressive steering. By maintaining non-zero speed it can be shown that under
certain assumptions the steering law (8.84) ensures that the origin of e(t) is UGAS and ULES for course
autopilots [FP06]. This only holds for heading autopilots if βc = 0, i.e., no environmental disturbances.
[BPP08] suggests a modified LOS guidance scheme for heading autopilots, Integral LOS (ILOS),
which includes integral action to compensate for non-zero βc and thereby account for environmental
forces which would otherwise steer the vessel off the path. The ILOS guidance scheme is given by
ψd(t) = αk −tan−1
e(t) + κeint(t)
∆(t)

,
∆> 0
(8.86a)
˙eint(t) =
∆(t)e(t)
(e(t) + eint(t))2 + ∆(t)2 ,
(8.86b)
where κ is an integral gain. The integral action allows ψd(t) to differ from the path tangent, αk, when
the vessel is on the path, e(t) = 0. Hence, when in the presence of disturbances that drive the vessel
away form the path, such as wind or currents, the integral of the cross-track error will generate the
necessary drift angle β to counteract βc and follow the path. [BPP08] also shows that the steering
law (8.86), under certain conditions, ensures that the origin of e(t) is UGAS and ULES for heading
autopilots. Among these conditions are the assumption of UGAS/ULES stability of the cascaded system
similar to (8.63) for the CB guidance law.
Since the path consists of multiple straight-line segments, a switching strategy between path way-
points needs to be defined in order for the vessel to correctly follow the path. For this purpose, consider
==PAGE:113==
8.5 Switching Control
106
a circle of acceptance with radius Rk+1 > 0 for waypoint pk+1. The switching criterion can then be
defined [Fos21]:
p
(xk+1 −x(t))2 + (yk+1 −y(t))2 ≤Rk+1,
(8.87)
This switching criterion states that if the vessels position p(t) is within the waypoint circle with radius
Rk+1, a switch occurs.
8.4.2.1
Applying LOS guided motion control
Consider the system (7.6)-(7.7) given by
˙η = R(η)(νr + νc),
(8.88a)
˙νr = f(η, νr) + τctrl.
(8.88b)
Now, assume that the ship is underactuated in the sense that it can only be controlled using force input
in surge and moment input in yaw, i.e.,
τctrl =


τu
0
τr

.
(8.89)
This means that the ship can only use the heading to control the direction of the velocity, i.e., a heading
autopilots is needed. As such, the ILOS scheme (8.86) can be used.
So far only the course and heading of the ship has been considered while requiring for the the ship
speed that U(t) > 0 in order for the ship to converge to the path. By associating a desired speed
Ud,k+1 > 0 to each waypoint pk+1 of the path, one may also control the speed at which the ship
traverses the path. The desired surge speed can then be chosen as [BPP08]
ud(t) = Ud,k+1 cos(ψd −αk).
(8.90)
Hence, as ψd approach αk the desired surge speed approaches the path speed. The desired sway speed
need to be chosen similar to αv,u in (8.76) in Section 8.4.1.2 in order for the velocity controller to behave
as if the ship was underactuated.
Using a route such as the ones generated in Section 6.3.2, one can then use this as a reference
path and the generated velocities associated with the route to compute the desired speed on the path.
It is then possible to employ the ILOS steering scheme (8.86), switching criterion (8.87) and desired
surge speed (8.90) for generating reference linear and angular velocities for a kinetic controller. For this
purpose, the controller derived in the CB section given by (8.69) can be used. It was shown that this
controller guarantees UGES of the velocity errors to the origin, and thereby the LOS ensures that the
origin of e(t) is UGES/ULES.
The LOS guided motion control system presented here will not be tested by itself. The LOS controller
is inherently designed to follow straight paths using only control input in surge and yaw and as such
cannot perform the desired turning manoeuvre reliably. This was also confirmed through experiments.
A switching control strategy will thus be used to switch between the LOS controller, for following
the reference route outside the turning area, and the integrator backstepping controller presented in
Section 8.2, for performing the turning manoeuvre. This will be elaborated in the Section 8.5 and in
Section 8.7.
8.5
Switching Control
Switching between control strategies can often be beneficial. Suppose two controllers have been designed;
controller A can follow a path but cannot perform a turning manoeuvre reliably, and controller B
capabilities are the opposite, i.e. it can turn reliably but cannot follow a path. Combining the two,
such that controller B performs the turning manoeuvre and controller A is used outside this manoeuvre,
could potentially complete the whole route without issues. The switching strategy used in this thesis is
==PAGE:114==
8.6 Executive Control Summary
107
simple yet adequate. Two points on the north axis are designated as switching lines. The northernmost
of these indicates the beginning of the turning manoeuvre, the other the end. The active controller is
switched when one of these is passed.
8.5.1
Bumpless Control Switching
Switching between controllers can lead to undesirable discontinuous jumps in the input signal. Bumpless
Control Switching (BCS) [Bla+16] is a mitigation strategy that produces smooth transitions between
control signals.
Let τ i be the input signal produced by the controller i, where i denotes either controller A or B.
BCS achieves a smooth transition between τ A and τ B by adding ”bump-states”, xb,i, to the signals:
τi = τ i + xb,i.
Here, xb,i obeys the dynamics
˙xb,i = Abxb,i + Lb (τ −τi) ,
(8.91)
where Ab is diagonal and negative definite, Lb is diagonal and positive definite, and τ is the signal
of the current active controller, i.e. τ = τA if controller A is active and vice-versa. The method is
best understood with an example. Suppose controller A is active and xb,A = 0 so τ = τA = τ A.
Furthermore, for simplicity, let τ A and τ B be constant but with different values. While B is inactive,
the corresponding bump dynamics (8.91) become
˙xb,B = Abxb,B + Lb (τ −τB)
= Abxb,B + Lb (τ A −τ B −xb,B)
= (Ab −Lb) xb,B + Lb (τ A −τ B) .
That is, a linear system with the difference between the control signals as input.
By construction,
(Ab −Lb) is Hurwitz, thereby xb,B will converge to a constant value equal to
x∗
b,B = −(Ab −Lb)−1 Lb (τ A −τ B) .
Clearly, it is desirable for
−(Ab −Lb)−1 Lb ≈I,
such that x∗
b,B approximates the difference between the signals.
This can be achieved by choosing
∥Ab∥≪∥Lb∥, in which case, τB will approach τA. Hence, when controller B becomes active, the signal
has no discontinuous jump. Furthermore, the final term in (8.91) vanishes, thereby τB will approach
τ B as xb,B converges to zero. Thus a smooth transition is achieved.

8.7
Results
The presented control strategies will now be evaluated and compared. Model predictive control did not
perform well enough to be imported into FORCE Technology’s simulator and will only be tested in
MATLAB. The other control systems will be evaluated based on their tracking performance in FORCE
Technology’s simulator. The constraints on the input and the change in input can be seen in Table 8.2.
The performance metrics used for evaluating and comparing control strategies are presented in the next
section.

8.7.1
Performance Metrics
The tracking performance of our control systems will be evaluated based on the following criteria:
• Tracking performance - measured in cross-track and along-track errors as described in Sec-
tion 8.4.2.
Only positional tracking is considered; including heading tracking would bias the
results against methods using the heading as a free variable, such as LOS. The only requirement
related to heading is that the ship actually performs the turning manoeuvre.
• Input use - both effectiveness and the amount thereof. Effectiveness is a qualitative assessment.
Examples of ineffective usage are applying the bow thrusters at high velocities, or fast changes in
input where a smooth signal could have achieved similar performance. The total actuation will
be measured in MATA, (6.38).
==PAGE:116==
8.7 Results
109
• Virtual inputs - qualitative assessment, where desirable characteristics are smooth, slowly vary-
ing and generally small virtual inputs. Note that smaller is not necessarily better. For example,
requesting surge and sway simultaneously can be infeasible for allocation, even if the amounts are
small.
8.7.2
Model Predictive Control Results
The test was performed on the high sway route as seen in Figure 6.10. Two tests were performed - one
without wind and one with wind. The wind was specified according to the analysis done in Section
4.3, i.e. 11 m/s from a direction of 217◦. A control horizon of N = 3 was used. The results for the
simulation without wind can be seen in Figure 8.7. Here it is seen that the model predictive controller
tracks the route well when just sailing straight. Later in the simulation, the controller manages to
perform the turning manoeuvre but struggles with performing it at the right time and place. This is
especially evident when considering the cross-track and along-track errors. In the turning manoeuvre
the errors exceed magnitudes of 50 meters.
The azipods are also seen to be moved towards port while moving out of the harbour and towards
the turning basin, which seems counter-intuitive. This would create a positive angular velocity that
would turn the ship in the opposite direction than desired. The bow thruster is also seen to be turned
on at the same time. This, along with the azipod thruster RPM being turned down, results in the turn
rate being minuscule.
The results for the simulation with wind can be seen in the appendix in Figure A.2. They will not
be analysed here as they are very similar to the results with no wind. The controller is able to handle
the given scenario while performing the turning manoeuvre similar to when no wind is present.
The Mean Absolute Thruster Actuation (MATA) can be seen in Table 8.3. Comparing it to the
input sequence of the high sway route in Table 6.3, MATA without wind for LMPC is seen to be
approximately 46% higher for the azipods and approximately 11% higher for the bow thruster. The
MATA for the simulation with wind is lower as the wind helps the ship move towards the reference.
LMPC

Wind

Table 8.3: MATA for the model predictive controller without and with wind.
The good results for when the ship is sailing straight might be explained by the linearisation being
a good approximation to the real model whenever the ship is not turning. On the other hand, the
linearisation might also explain the poor results when turning. Here the linearisation becomes especially
bad as the nonlinear terms are poorly approximated by linear expressions for larger changes in argument.
The poor approximation also explains the small control horizon, N = 3. The control horizon was chosen
small as the performance of the controller degraded when chosen larger. A nonlinear model along with
a larger control horizon might have provided better results. Using the larger control horizon could
potentially enable the controller to use its predictive abilities to the fullest extent. For this to be viable,
one would have to find solutions to the computational performance aspect of the optimisation problem
in each iteration.

8.7.3
Simulating in FORCE Technology’s Simulator
The comparison in FORCE Technology’s simulator will be conducted in two rounds. First, all four
strategies will be compared without wind. Then, the best-performing strategies will be identified and
consequently compared with wind present as described in Section 4.3. Each control strategy will be
evaluated as a combined control system with the predictive allocator tracking the high sway reference
seen in Figure 6.14.
As will be evident, most of the presented strategies are challenged by under-
actuation at higher velocities. Hence, one switching control strategy will be applied. In the formulation
of Section 8.5, the LOS design as controller A is combined with the integrator backstepping as controller
B. The switching lines are set to
Nswitch,1 = −35 and Nswitch,2 = −220.
These two control strategies are only evaluated as a combined switching control system referred to as
LOS-IB. The other systems will be referred to using the following abbreviations:
Full-state feedback PID - PID
Sliding mode control - SMC
Constant bearing - CB
Throughout the comparison, the reference is separated into three phases: before, during and after the
turning manoeuvre. The time intervals for these are, in order, 0 to 250 seconds, 250 to 400 seconds and
400 to 586 seconds.
8.7.3.1
Handling Bow Thruster Mismatch
Section 5.3.1.1 demonstrated that the identified model overestimates and underestimates the capabilities
of the bow thruster at respectively high and low velocities. Potential solutions were also presented, but
these could not be implemented within the project’s time frame. Instead, this thesis applies a partial
solution, where the allocator’s parameters are functions of the surge velocity such that bow thruster
usage is discouraged at high velocities. This reduces unnecessary usage at high velocities but does not
address the effects of underestimation.
The penalty on bow thruster actuation is chosen as a quadratic function of the velocity,
P3,3 = 1 + 2ur + 2u2
r.
This limits the bow thruster usage at high velocities, but the allocator still attempts to produce sway
at high velocities by ineffective means. To deter such behaviour, the slack penalty on sway is chosen as
a linear interpolation between a minimum and maximum value,
Q2,2 = (1 −ρ)qmin + ρqmax,
where
ρ = 1 −tanh
1
8u4
r

.
(8.92)
qmax is chosen such that the allocator actively produces sway at low speed and qmin is chosen such that
it does not at high speed. This is achieved by choosing 0 < qmin < qmax, with qmin large enough such
that excessive sway is not produced by fulfilling surge and yaw at high speed. The purpose of (8.92) is
similar to (8.74) as it reflects the ability to produce sway at high speed. Hence, the penalty on sway
slack in the allocator is indirectly a function of the simulator ship’s ability to produce sway.
8.7.3.2
Results in FORCE Technology’s Simulator
Simulating each of the control systems (PID, SMC, LOS-IB and CB) in FORCE Technology’s simulator
gives the trajectories in Figure 8.8, with corresponding tracking errors in Figure 8.9. Furthermore, actual
==PAGE:119==
8.7 Results
112
input signals and virtual input signals are included in Figure 8.10 and Figure 8.11 respectively. Table 8.4
quantifies the input usage for each control system. Considering Figure 8.9, both PID and SMC deviate
from the reference in Figure 8.8, leading to high cross-track errors. These systems assume that sway
can be produced at all velocities and attempt to correct their course with negative virtual input in sway
as seen in Figure 8.11. However, sway can only be produced at lower velocities, and both strategies
accumulate a large allocation error in the corresponding virtual input channel. Apart from deviating
in position, these strategies are in other aspects generally well-behaved. Their other virtual inputs are
smooth, slowly varying and successfully fulfilled by the allocator. Considering the thruster inputs in
Figure 8.10, the SMC system applies the bow thruster outside the turning manoeuvre but otherwise
has effective input usage. In particular, it has the least amount of azipod usage according to MATA
of all systems compared. In comparison, the PID system’s inputs are not quite as effective and have
chatter in both the azipods’ RPM and angles.

Considering Figure 8.8 again, the LOS-IB and CB systems show better tracking performance than
the PID and SMC. Seen in Figure 8.9, their cross-track errors are close to zero throughout most of the
transit, with only slight bumps during the turning manoeuvre. Furthermore, their along-track errors
converge to zero at the end, so they both complete the reference on time. Arriving on time is not actively
pursued by LOS-IB since it lacks a catch-up mechanic; it arriving on time here can be attributed to good
parameter values for this route. From Figure 8.10, both systems show effective bow thruster usage, only
applying them when the velocity is low. When the bow thrusters are utilised, the CB system activates
and deactivates them in rapid succession, whereas the LOS-IB system shows smoother but overall higher
usage. Regarding the azipods, the starboard one is heavily utilised by the LOS-IB system, while the
azipods are more evenly utilised by the CB system. The LOS-IB system oscillates the azipods’ thrust
after completing the turning manoeuvre. Experience shows that this behaviour is attempting to correct
the yaw velocity, though the effectiveness is debatable, and adjusting the azipod angles would have
been preferable. The CB system does something similar at the end of the simulation, where it attempts
to correct the yaw velocity by deactivating the starboard azipod and fully activating the portside one.
The CB system is more aggressive in its azipod usage during the turning manoeuvre, with fast changes
in both azipods’ direction and thrust. The aggressiveness may be explained by the virtual inputs from
the CB system seen in Figure 8.11. Here, it is seen that the virtual input in yaw reaches an excessive
magnitude during the turning manoeuvre, almost four times larger than observed in the other systems.
In contrast, the virtual inputs from the LOS-IB system are close in magnitude to those produced by
PID and SMC. Nevertheless, the virtual inputs from both the LOS-IB and CB systems are generally
fulfilled, and neither produces virtual input in sway outside the turning manoeuvre. It was found that by
relaxing a few of the controller gains in the CB system, smoother input signals could be achieved. Plots
are included in the appendix, Figure A.3. This led to larger tracking errors, but the CB system would
still approach and rendezvous with the reference at the end point, illustrating its catch-up capabilities.
A summary of the comparisons between control systems can be seen in Table 8.6.

value [JFB04], so the thruster’s mean power consumption along a traversal consisting of K steps can
be quantified by

The PID and SMC systems deviate too far from the reference to constitute reliable tracking. This
could be alleviated using a switching strategy similar to the LOS-IB combination, but this was not
==PAGE:121==
8.8 Proposed Control Solution
114
feasible within the project’s time frame. In contrast, the LOS-IB and CB systems both tracked the
reference with only brief and small deviations. Hence, these systems will move on to the second round
and be compared with wind. Simulating the mentioned systems with wind gives the trajectories and
corresponding tracking errors in Figure A.4. Actual and virtual inputs are included in Figure A.5, and
the input usage is quantified in Table 8.4. Both systems show almost identical tracking performance with
only minor behavioural differences, most notably that their allocated inputs are now more aggressive
compared to those without winds.
8.8
Proposed Control Solution
The comparison in Section 8.7.3 did not conclude with an obvious best-performing control system but
identified two systems that could reliably track the reference: the LOS-IB and CB systems. Of these,
LOS-IB had the smoothest inputs during the turning manoeuvre while still following the reference
path. However, it lacks a catch-up mechanic, and would often complete the reference behind schedule.
The considered route is quite short, only stretching about 1.5 km measured vertically. As such, the
delays observed in testing were only minor, but major delays could occur on longer routes. Although
this issue is not present within the CB system, its aggressiveness during the turning manoeuvre was
deemed excessive, and if relaxed to achieve smoother input signals, it would often deviate from the path
when trying to rendezvous with the reference. The project thus proposes a switching control system
combining the CB strategy with the LOS-IB strategy, exploiting the advantages of each control strat-
egy. Combining the control strategies would utilise the LOS guidance from LOS-IB and CB guidance
for a guided motion controller achieving both path following and target tracking outside the turning
manoeuvre, while maintaining smooth input signals. A similar control strategy has already been sug-
gested in [BSF06], and the proposed combination of LOS and CB guidance should therefore be readily
implementable from the theory already presented with a few tweaks. During the turning manoeuvre
the integrator backstepping controller from LOS-IB is still to be used as it has shown the most reliable
tracking capabilities in the turn. The system would apply a similar bumpless switching strategy as
in LOS-IB, i.e., the switch between control strategies occurs when the ship enters/leaves the specified
turning region within the turning basin.

CHAPTER 9
Future Work
• Decision Support:
– The proposed solution could also be used for decision support for inexperienced captains or
in a training environment. The system would suggest control signals consisting of handle
placements and wheel orders based on current position and where you want to go.
The
proposed solution would have to be altered such that the control signals consisting of RPM
and thruster angles were converted to handle placements and wheel orders that the captain
could implement. The commands would also need to be less varying and potentially piecewise
constant such that the captains could follow the signal without much issue. Otherwise, the
captains would use too much effort on following the proposed control signal and loose focus
on their task. This could be implemented in the control allocator directly. The alteration
of the structure of the control signals would lead to the controllers needing to be tuned
differently.
• Minimum Data Requirement:
– One could also consider the minimum amount of data required to estimate a suitable model
of the ship using the system identification framework presented in this thesis. A proposed
solution is by using the simulator as a proxy for the real ship. Experiments in the simulator
could indicate what the necessary and suﬀicient tests would be. In general, early testing
showed that the input signal would need to be suﬀiciently excited and separated.
The
former would ensure the proper input dynamics was captured while the latter would ensure
that the individual control inputs could be discerned.
• Robustness and Fault Tolerance:
– As the real world is inherently unpredictable, it would be wise to consider the effects of
something unexpected happening. This could be everything from a noisy or entirely lost
GPS signal to faults in the engine or the azipods. For a noisy or entirely lost GPS signal one
could use a state estimator or use other sensors, e.g. radar, inertial navigation system, ship
log, depth transducer or similar. Faults in the engine or azipods would require a dynamically
changing control allocator, i.e. T and bounds on n and α would change dynamically.
• Extension of Model Dynamics:
– The estimated control model of M/F Copenhagen only captures dynamics related to the
azipods and bow thrusters. The ship itself is also equipped with a rudder, a propeller and a
rotor sail. The first two are relatively simple additions, while the rotor sail is more involved.
Here one would need to model the forces produced as with changing RPM of the rotor sail
and the relative wind direction and speed. The azipod-azipod, azipod-propeller and azipod-
rudder interactions, caused mainly by the interaction of the flow wash, could also be modelled.
This would all have to be included in the control allocator.
==PAGE:127==
CHAPTER 10
Conclusion
The project’s objective was to develop an autonomous system for departing M/F Copenhagen. To this
end, a data-driven model has been estimated and subsequently used to develop an offline guidance
system and a control system. As illustrated in Figure 10.1, the project solution has been separated into
model estimation, guidance system and control system.
Control Law
CB-LOS-IB
Guidance System
RRT + MBOR
Weather Data
Control System
Control Allocator
Predictive Allocator
Ship / Ship
Simulator
Model Estimation
Support Vector
Regression
Transit Data
Guidance and Control System
Online
Offline
Figure 10.1: Project Solution to In-Port Ferry Manoeuvring Problem.
A 3 DOF mathematical model of a surface vessel was developed based on the ship specifics of
M/F Copenhagen and inspired by the kinematic equations and rigid-body kinetic equations for surface
vessels developed by T. Fossen in [Fos21]. These included models for the actuators present on M/F
Copenhagen such as azipods and bow thrusters. Furthermore, both a detailed and simplified model of
the outline of Gedser Harbour and the turning basin outside Gedser Harbour were developed for use in
system identification, guidance and control.
Parameters in the mathematical model were identified to accurately describe M/F Copenhagen. This
identification was based on transit data from M/F Berlin provided by DTU and FORCE Technology,
which was processed to better suit the goals of the project. The processing included fixing interpolation-
errors, computing distances to banks and limiting the data to be local to Gedser.
To accurately estimate the parameters describing the M/F Copenhagen, a support vector regression
(SVR) method was used. The SVR method was compared to a least square (LS) method; the models
estimated by each method yielded similar accuracy in predictions, but the model given by the SVR
method slightly outperformed the LS method. The identified SVR model had the largest prediction
errors during the turning manoeuvre, and a model estimated using sparse identification of nonlinear
dynamics (SINDy) was appended to the SVR model to potentially increase the overall accuracy. Two
different SINDy models were trained on the prediction errors of the SVR model and each sought to
capture dynamics related to manoeuvring in restricted waters such as squat and bank effects, which
were not modelled in the mathematical model the SVR was trained on. Both SINDy models yielded
==PAGE:128==
10 Conclusion
121
only minor increases in accuracy at the cost of a large increase in model complexity. It was believed
that the little improvement in prediction accuracy provided by the SINDy models was caused by either
the squat and bank effects not being suﬀiciently present in the data, or that these effects were already
captured by the SVR model and the inaccuracies of the SVR model would then have been caused by
other factors. It was thus concluded that none of the SINDy models provided a significant increase in
model precision for the increase in model complexity to be justified. The idea of using SINDy to model
the uncaptured dynamics in the SVR model was therefore discarded in the following work.
From tests, it was further concluded that the SVR model estimated from the actual transit data did
not adequately describe the ship simulated in FORCE Technology’s simulator. A new SVR model was
identified using data generated from the FORCE simulator as a solution. It was concluded that the
modelled azipods and bow thrusters were the main contributors to the model / simulator difference. The
data included additional tests isolating the effect of the azipods and bow thrusters to resolve this issue.
Even though these were included, it was found that the identified model still generally underestimated
the bow thrusters’ effectiveness at low velocities and overestimated their effectiveness at high velocities,
when compared to FORCE Technology’s simulator.
For the guidance system, methods within RRT and dynamical optimisation were explored. RRT
could find routes in a nonconvex setting but showed erratic behaviour unfeasible for the real ship. This
was concluded to stem from wrong implementation in code and a poor choice of model and accompanying
controller. Correcting these mistakes could alleviate these problems. MBOR proved to be a flexible
approach to offline guidance, allowing near-endless possibilities for inducing desired behaviour along
the reference. However, the optimiser abuses model inaccuracies, particularly the identified model’s
mismatch in the bow thrusters. Consequently, the references generated are deemed infeasible to track
in both position and velocity. Tracking in position only is feasible, albeit with some minor overshoots
due to velocity mismatches. On its own, MBOR converged slowly. An initial route for the optimisation
algorithm within MBOR was generated using the RRT method, which improved the eﬀiciency.
Control allocation introduced virtual inputs to simplify the identified model, as the model itself was
unsuitable for control design. Two optimal allocation strategies were presented to allocate the virtual
inputs: singularity avoidance allocation (SSA) and predictive allocation (PA). Through testing, it was
found that PA outperformed SSA. This comparison was made by considering the general behaviour of
input signals and the amount of input produced for each allocator.
Six control strategies were considered: full-state PID (PID), sliding mode (SMC), integrator back-
stepping (IB), linear model predictive control (LMPC), line-of-sight (LOS) and constant bearing (CB).
LMPC was only tested in a MATLAB simulation using the identified model, whereas PID, SMC, IB,
LOS and CB were tested in the FORCE Technology’s simulator. LMPC did not need the control allo-
cator but was not able to track the reference reliably in the MATLAB simulation environment. It was
therefore concluded that the linearisation was insuﬀicient in describing the dynamics of the ship.
PID is a simple and well established control strategy. However, it had the false assumption of being
able to produce sway at all velocities. This resulted in deviations from the reference when tracking in
FORCE Technology’s simulator.
In general, SMC is robust against uncertainties and disturbances with known bounds under the
assumption that virtual inputs are fulfilled. The control strategy had the same false assumption as PID,
which resulted in similar deviations from the reference.
The LOS and IB strategies were combined into a switching control strategy (LOS-IB) to relax the
false assumption that sway can be produced at all velocities. The switching strategy in LOS-IB worked
by using IB during the turning manoeuvre and LOS otherwise.
The LOS-IB system showed good
tracking performance throughout the whole reference in FORCE Technology’s simulator, but it lacked
a catch-up mechanic, which could lead to delays. Such a catch-up/rendezvous mechanic was inherent to
CB and it was able to adequately follow the reference. However, a downside to CB was that it suffered
from aggressive and excessive inputs during the turning manoeuvre.
Based on these observations, a final switching control system was proposed consisting of a combina-
tion of LOS-IB and CB denoted CB-LOS-IB. The proposed control solution would utilise a combined
CB and LOS guided motion control outside the turning manoeuvre. This would potentially achieve
both path following and target tracking outside the turning manoeuvre. To perform the turning ma-
==PAGE:129==
10 Conclusion
122
noeuvre the integrator backstepping strategy would be used. CB-LOS-IB would thus utilise the pros of
each combined control strategy to make up for the cons.
==PAGE:130==
APPENDIX A
Appendix
A.1
Lyapunov Stability Theory
This section briefly presents the aspects of Lyapunov stability theory that are relevant to this thesis,
particularly the classification of equilibrium points and the use of Lyapunov functions to prove stability.
The theory presented is taken from [Kha02], which the reader is referred to for an extensive overview
of the subject.
Lyapunov stability theory concerns the behaviour of dynamical systems in proximity of equilibrium
points. The theory is built around systems on the form:
˙x = fx(x),
(A.1)
x(0) = x0,
where x = 0 is an equilibrium point and f is a locally Lipschitz on some domain. This does not restrict
the theory to equilibrium points at the origin; if xe is an arbitrary equilibrium point of (A.1), then
˙z = fz(z),
z(0) = z0,
with z = x −xe is on the form (A.1) and has an equilibrium point at 0. Lyapunov’s theory itself is
only concerned with equilibrium points and does not consider inputs. Hence, (A.1) may be interpreted
as a closed-loop system.
A.1.1
Stability of Equilibrium Points
Lyapunov classifies equilibrium points according to the system’s behaviour when in proximity to the
points. These classifications form a hierarchy and will be presented from least to most restrictive.
Definition 1 The equilibrium point x = 0 of (A.1) is stable if for every R > 0 there exist r > 0 such
that
∥x(0)∥< r ⇒∥x(t)∥< R, ∀t ≥0.
(A.2)
The point is unstable if it is not stable.
A stable equilibrium point is also referred to as being marginally stable when distinction from other forms
of stability is necessary. Definition 1 has a clear interpretation: any trajectory initialised suﬀiciently
close to the equilibrium point stays close for all future time.
Definition 2 The equilibrium point x = 0 of (A.1) is locally asymptotically stable if it is stable and
there exists a ρ > 0 such that
∥x(0)∥< ρ ⇒lim
t→∞∥x(t)∥= 0.
(A.3)
The point is globally asymptotically stable if (A.3) is satisfied for all ρ ∈R+.
Asymptotic stability is marginal stability with a convergence guarantee. If the stability is global, then
any trajectory, no matter how far away, will converge to the equilibrium point as time goes towards
infinity.
==PAGE:131==
A.1 Lyapunov Stability Theory
124
Definition 3 The equilibrium point x = 0 of (A.1) is locally exponentially stable if there exist c, a, λ > 0
such that
∥x(0)∥< c ⇒∥x(t)∥≤a ∥x(t)∥e−λt, ∀t ≥0.
(A.4)
The point is globally exponentially stable if (A.4) is satisfied for all c ∈R+.
As opposed to asymptotic stability, exponential stability guarantees a specific rate of convergence to
the equilibrium point. This is the strongest form of stability achievable for most systems without using
discontinuous control.
A.1.2
Lyapunov Functions
Lyapunov theory uses Lyapunov functions to classify the stability properties of equilibrium points.
These can be interpreted as measuring the energy contained in the system, an interpretation that will
be used throughout.
Definition 4 A continuously differentiable function V : Ω→R defined on a domain Ωcontaining
x = 0 is a Lyapunov function if:
V (0) = 0,
(A.5)
V (x) > 0, ∀x ∈Ω−0,
(A.6)
˙V (x) ≤0, ∀x ∈Ω−0
(A.7)
A function for which condition (A.7) is yet to be proven is referred to as a candidate Lyapunov function,
e.g. the quadratic function:
V (x) = 1
2xT Γx,
(A.8)
where Γ is positive definite. Candidate functions on the form (A.8) are often used in practice.
Theorem 1 The equilibrium point x = 0 is stable if a Lyapunov function exists.
Condition (A.7) ensures more energy is never gained. Thus, the system must stay in proximity to the
equilibrium point.
Theorem 2 The equilibrium point x = 0 is locally asymptotically stable if there exists a Lyapunov
function, V , satisfying that
˙V (x) < 0, ∀x ∈Ω−0.
(A.9)
The point is globally asymptotically stable if Ω= Rn and V further satisfies that
∥x∥→∞⇒V (x) →∞.
(A.10)
The more strict condition (A.9) ensures energy always dissipates, thereby the system must converge to
the equilibrium point. Functions satisfying (A.10) are said to be radially unbounded. Notice that (A.8)
is radially unbounded.
Theorem 3 The equilibrium point x = 0 is locally exponentially stable if there exists a Lyapunov
function, V , satisfying that
k1 ∥x∥a ≤V (x) ≤k2 ∥x∥a ,
(A.11)
˙V (x) ≤−k3 ∥x∥a ,
(A.12)
on the whole domain, Ω, and with positive constants k1,k2,k3 and a. The point is globally exponentially
stable if Ω= Rn.
==PAGE:132==
A.1 Lyapunov Stability Theory
125
Notice the previous theorems only present necessary conditions for different forms of stability. Thus,
failing to find a suitable Lyapunov function does not prove instability. Condition (A.12) ensures energy
dissipates fast enough to guarantee exponential convergence. One case where exponentially is trivial to
prove (or disprove) is linear systems.
Lemma 1 The equilibrium point x = 0 of the linear system:
˙x = Ax,
is globally exponentially stable if and only if A is negative definite.
Lemma 2 Suppose there exist symmetric and positive definite matrices, Γ1 and Γ2, such that V (x) =
xT Γ1x is a Lyapunov function whose derivative satisfies
˙V (x) ≤−xT Γ2x.
Then, the equilibrium point x = 0 is globally exponentially stable. Moreover, the conditions in Theorem 3
are satisfied with:
k1 ≤λmin (Γ1) ,
k2 ≥λmax (Γ1) ,
k3 ≤λmin (Γ2) .
Proof: Because Γ1 and Γ2 are symmetric and positive definite matrices, all their eigenvalues are
real and positive. Furthermore
λmin (Γ1) ∥x∥2

and
−xT Γ2x ≤−λmin (Γ2) ∥x∥2

Hence, the conditions in Theorem 3 are satisfied with:
k1 ≤λmin (Γ1) ,
k2 ≥λmax (Γ1) ,
k3 ≤λmin (Γ2) .
Thus, x = 0 is globally exponentially stable. □

invariant set [EL91].
The interpretation here is that if the sliding variable satisfies the sliding condition, it will converge to
zero in finite time. The sliding variable will also stay equal to zero for all future times.
